        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.1c04482"><meta property="og:title" content="Discharge State of Layered P2-Type Cathode Reveals Unsafe than Charge Condition in Thermal Runaway Event for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/acsami.1c04482.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="A sol–gel process followed by heat treatment derived a layered P2-type NaCoO2 cathode, which depicted unit cell parameters values of a = 2.8389 Å, c = 10.9899 Å, and V = 76.71 Å3 in powder X-ray diffraction pattern. The synthesized cathode exhibited hexagonal, 2D platelets with an ∼300 nm thickness. During the anodic and cathodic sweeps, the cyclic voltammograms revealed multiple redox peaks with the same current densities, shapes, and peak positions, associated with the highly reversible phase transition mechanism of the layered P2-type NaCoO2 cathode. The sodium cells yielded the capacities of 93/92 mAh g–1 at 0.5 C and 87/87 mAh g–1 at 1 C for the 50th charge–discharge cycles. The in situ multimode calorimetry (MMC) studies of sodium cells demonstrated a thermal explosion event, which occurred by sodium melting, short-circuit, electrode decomposition reaction, gas generation, exothermic reaction, released heat energy ,and cell gasket melting. Ultimately, the calculated released total heat energies of ∼550/740 J g–1 for in situ MMC studies and ∼312/594 J g–1 for ex situ DSC analyses (charge state at 4 V and discharge state at 2 V) show that the discharged state of sodiated layered P2-type NaCoO2 cathode material is more unsafe than the charge state. Furthermore, the ex situ differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) spectrum of a discharge state at 2 V of layered P2-type NaCoO2 revealed a decreased onset temperature (DOT) at 141 °C with two pronounced exothermic peaks at 197 and 266 °C with a released higher total heat energy of 594 J g–1 than the charge state heat energy at 312 J g–1, attributed to the higher charge onset temperature (COT) at 191 °C. Thus, the observed higher heat energy and decreased onset temperature for the discharge state at 2 V is associated with the higher Na+ ion in the discharge state of the layered P2-type NaxCoO2 cathode than that of the pristine cathode, showcasing that the layered P2-type NaCoO2 cathode is unsafe at the discharged condition for sodium-ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/acsami.1c04482.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6e38de9e7775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e38de9e7775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '7b049c47-9f2e-4993-a4aa-eaa3e7efd37f'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90f6e38de9e7775c-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e38de9e7775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="90f6e38de9e7775c-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Discharge State of Layered P2-Type Cathode Reveals Unsafe than Charge Condition in Thermal Runaway Event for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c04482", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Discharge State of Layered P2-Type Cathode Reveals Unsafe than Charge Condition in Thermal Runaway Event for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c04482", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Discharge State of Layered P2-Type Cathode Reveals Unsafe than Charge Condition in Thermal Runaway Event for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c04482", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Discharge State of Layered P2-Type Cathode Reveals Unsafe than Charge Condition in Thermal Runaway Event for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c04482", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Discharge State of Layered P2-Type Cathode Reveals Unsafe than Charge Condition in Thermal Runaway Event for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c04482", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Discharge State of Layered P2-Type Cathode Reveals Unsafe than Charge Condition in Thermal Runaway Event for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c04482", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Discharge State of Layered P2-Type Cathode Reveals Unsafe than Charge Condition in Thermal Runaway Event for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c04482", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Discharge State of Layered P2-Type Cathode Reveals Unsafe than Charge Condition in Thermal Runaway Event for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c04482", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Discharge State of Layered P2-Type Cathode Reveals Unsafe than Charge Condition in Thermal Runaway Event for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c04482", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

property="articleSection">research-article</div><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D31594%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DManikandan%2BPalanisamy%252C%2BVenkata%2BRami%2BReddy%2BBoddu%252C%2BParasharam%2BM.%2BShirage%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D27%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c04482%26title%3DDischarge%2BState%2Bof%2BLayered%2BP2-Type%2BCathode%2BReveals%2BUnsafe%2Bthan%2BCharge%2BCondition%2Bin%2BThermal%2BRunaway%2BEvent%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D31604%26publicationDate%3DJuly%2B2021" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/medium/am1c04482_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c04482&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">A sol–gel process followed by heat treatment derived a layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode, which depicted unit cell parameters values of <i>a</i> = 2.8389 Å, <i>c</i> = 10.9899 Å, and <i>V</i> = 76.71 Å<sup>3</sup> in powder X-ray diffraction pattern. The synthesized cathode exhibited hexagonal, 2D platelets with an ∼300 nm thickness. During the anodic and cathodic sweeps, the cyclic voltammograms revealed multiple redox peaks with the same current densities, shapes, and peak positions, associated with the highly reversible phase transition mechanism of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode. The sodium cells yielded the capacities of 93/92 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 C and 87/87 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 C for the 50th charge–discharge cycles. The <i>in situ</i> multimode calorimetry (MMC) studies of sodium cells demonstrated a thermal explosion event, which occurred by sodium melting, short-circuit, electrode decomposition reaction, gas generation, exothermic reaction, released heat energy ,and cell gasket melting. Ultimately, the calculated released total heat energies of ∼550/740 J g<sup>–1</sup> for <i>in situ</i> MMC studies and ∼312/594 J g<sup>–1</sup> for <i>ex situ</i> DSC analyses (charge state at 4 V and discharge state at 2 V) show that the discharged state of sodiated layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material is more unsafe than the charge state. Furthermore, the <i>ex situ</i> differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) spectrum of a discharge state at 2 V of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> revealed a decreased onset temperature (DOT) at 141 °C with two pronounced exothermic peaks at 197 and 266 °C with a released higher total heat energy of 594 J g<sup>–1</sup> than the charge state heat energy at 312 J g<sup>–1</sup>, attributed to the higher charge onset temperature (COT) at 191 °C. Thus, the observed higher heat energy and decreased onset temperature for the discharge state at 2 V is associated with the higher Na<sup>+</sup> ion in the discharge state of the layered P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> cathode than that of the pristine cathode, showcasing that the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode is unsafe at the discharged condition for sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D31594%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DManikandan%2BPalanisamy%252C%2BVenkata%2BRami%2BReddy%2BBoddu%252C%2BParasharam%2BM.%2BShirage%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D27%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c04482%26title%3DDischarge%2BState%2Bof%2BLayered%2BP2-Type%2BCathode%2BReveals%2BUnsafe%2Bthan%2BCharge%2BCondition%2Bin%2BThermal%2BRunaway%2BEvent%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D31604%26publicationDate%3DJuly%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290780&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Differential scanning calorimetry" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Differential%20scanning%20calorimetry" class="article__tags__link">Differential scanning calorimetry</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrochemical%20cells" class="article__tags__link">Electrochemical cells</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292506&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Materials" class="article__tags__link">Materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sodium" class="article__tags__link">Sodium</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Layered+P2-type+Cathode+Material&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_layered%20P2-type%20cathode%20material">layered P2-type cathode material</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Cell&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20cell">sodium cell</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Stable+Charge%E2%88%92discharge+Cycle&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_stable%20charge%E2%88%92discharge%20cycle">stable charge−discharge cycle</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Higher+Exothermic+Heat+Energy&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_higher%20exothermic%20heat%20energy">higher exothermic heat energy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Safety&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_safety">safety</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The advancement in renewable energy sources is a leading factor for global development and sustainable green environments. Because, the primary nonrenewable energy sources (fossil fuels) of coal, oil, gas, etc., are having critical environmental impacts due to the emission of toxic gases SO<sub>2</sub>, SO<sub>3</sub>, CO, CO<sub>2</sub>, etc., toward global climate change. Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are the most attractive renewable energy storage sources in the place of fossil fuels and are useful in a grid energy storage infrastructure, which stores power obtained from solar and wind sources. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Since 1990, LIBs have governed as the predominant energy storage devices in various applications such as communication, transportation, and stationary energy storage for the reasons of high energy density, long cycle life, and lightweight portable energy source compared to other rechargeable batteries such as Pb–PbO<sub>2</sub>, Ni–Cd, Ni–MH, and Ag–Zn. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 3 and 4">(3,4)</a> Therefore, LIBs have been stimulated to massive production with an advanced charging protocol using microprocessor technology for use in high-power applications, particularly electric vehicles (EVs), hybrid electric vehicles (HEVs), and grid energy storage systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref4" aria-label="reference 1, 2 and 4">(1,2,4)</a> However, the availability of lithium sources is very limited and expensive to continuous scale up for high-power applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 5 to 7">(5−7)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Accordingly, the research and development are focused on alternative energy storage systems, viz., sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and its technologies, being more cost-effective due to abundant sodium resources and geographically distributed throughout the world. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a> The concentration of sodium in the earth’s crust and seawater is much higher than that of lithium and offers low-cost SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 10 to 13">(10−13)</a> The meriting SIB advantages with an operating cell voltage &gt;4 V, high power density, and energy density are pointed out as a high-performance sodium-ion energy storage technology to move to large-scale production for high-power applications (EVs, HEVs, and grid energy-storage systems). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14,15)</a> Thus, the pioneering studies of hard carbon related to the intercalation–deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ion characteristics <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 16 to 18">(16−18)</a> and its thermal safety aspects <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> have been recognized as a superior anode and yielded the discharge–charge capacities of 413/321 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for SIBs. Significantly, the cathode delivers Na<sup>+</sup> ions to the hard carbon anode during charge–discharge cycles and defines the cell voltage of SIBs. Hence, the important sodium-ion cathode materials are layered transition metal oxides (represented by Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MO<sub>2</sub> type) Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> NaCrO<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>M<sub><i>y</i></sub>M<sub><i>z</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> and polyanionic systems NaFePO<sub>4</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> NaVPO<sub>4</sub>F, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 29 and 30">(29,30)</a> and NaFeSO<sub>4</sub>F. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> For more than four decades, significant efforts have been put forward to study the development of layered transition metal oxides, which are exhibited as a promising cathode material with rich structural properties, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 32 to 36">(32−36)</a> described by a general formula of A<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> (A = alkali, TM = transition metal). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref20 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref20 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 15, 20 and 37 to 41">(15,20,37−41)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The most common sodium-ion cathode material is NaCoO<sub>2</sub>, for the reason that the similar layered LiCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material has been successfully implemented for lithium-ion battery (LIB) applications, which are easily synthesized with the layered structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The layered sodium cobalt oxide (NaCoO<sub>2</sub>) is built by a CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and formed the stable structure of O3-, P3-, or P2-type (3 or 2 indicate the repeating unit cell in the layered structure), wherein the intercalation and deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions occur between CoO<sub>6</sub> sheets related to the octahedra (O) or prismatic (P) environments. Among these types, the layered P2-type material has been exhibited as a promising cathode material for SIBs owing to high-energy density and long cycle life. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> In this P2-type structure, the oxygen stacking is denoted in AB|BA close-packed arrangement, while the O3-type structure of LiTMO<sub>2</sub> adopted an ABCABC arrangement. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> This significant change in the crystal structure might reveal different types of structural changes in the NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode during charge–discharge cycles, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> compared to the well-known LiCoO<sub>2</sub> system. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Accordingly, layered P2-type cathode materials are regarded as an attractive candidate rather than O3- and P3-type structures for SIBs <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref41 ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref41 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 15, 41, 47 and 48">(15,41,47,48)</a> and can observe different types of reversible structural phase transitions during Na<sup>+</sup> ion intercalation and deintercalation processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref46" aria-label="reference 20 and 46">(20,46)</a> Besides, the layered P2-type structure exhibits two different sites for Na<sup>+</sup> ions, viz., 2b site sharing two faces with MO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and 2d site sharing the edges with MO<sub>6</sub> octahedra. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref41 ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref41 ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 15, 41, 47 and 48">(15,41,47,48)</a> Thus, the pioneering research studies on Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> and its substituted Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>1–<i>y</i></sub>M<sub><i>y</i></sub>Ni<sub><i>z</i></sub>FeO<sub>2</sub> cathode materials delivered a specific capacity of 120–150 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and showed promising performance for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref25 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref25 ref49" aria-label="reference 46, 25 and 49">(46,25,49)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Although SIBs have been developed as a superior alternative system in the place of LIBs, the thermal safety aspects of SIBs are questionable, because sodium metal is highly reactive and more flammable than lithium metal. Hence, investigation on the thermal properties of sodium-ion cathodes is very important for them to reach the market and high-power applications. At a high temperature, the charged lithium-ion cathodes of the layered LiTMO<sub>2</sub> type release O<sub>2</sub> gas, which reacts with an organic electrolyte, making the whole battery system extremely dangerous. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 50 and 51">(50,51)</a> Thus, the thermal safety characteristics of layered NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material are required to be examined at elevated temperatures for SIBs. The thermal safety characteristics of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> and NaFeO<sub>2</sub> cathode materials were studied by accelerating rate calorimetry (ARC), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> time-resolved X-ray diffraction, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> high-temperature synchrotron X-ray diffraction, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> and differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) techniques. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> Moreover, the reactivity of deintercalated P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material was investigated by ARC and showed that the deintercalated Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> cathode is highly reactive with a solvent and electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> Using the <i>in situ</i> transmission electron microscopic (TEM) technique, the thermal stability of the P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> cathode was studied from room temperature to 400 °C and the presence of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, CoO, and Co and the loss of oxygen were confirmed. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> Nevertheless, the realistic thermal safety aspects of layered NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material in sodium-metal/sodium-ion batteries have not been fully evaluated for high-power applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref58" aria-label="reference 46 and 58">(46,58)</a> Therefore, a research study is required on the basis of the thermal safety characteristics of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material was synthesized by the sol–gel method and the phase purity by powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) pattern was confirmed. The synthesized material exhibited a flaky-bundles morphology and yielded stable charge–discharge capacities of 93/92 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 C and 87/87 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 C for the 50th cycle. Rate studies showed remarkable stable performance and delivered the charge–discharge capacities of 71/70 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 C. With this remarkable electrochemical performance, the realistic thermal safety aspects of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material were examined in a sodium coin cell (comprising NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode, sodium metal, electrolyte, separator, etc.) using an <i>in situ</i> multimode calorimetry (MMC) operando technique, which directly reveals the thermal explosion events for the charged and discharged sodium cells as a function of temperature increment from room temperature to 300 °C. Further, the thermal stabilities of fully charged/discharged layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material were investigated by <i>ex situ</i> differential scanning calorimetry studies.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Structural Investigation of Layered P2-Type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> Cathode Material</h3><div class="NLM_p">Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) of the sol–gel precursor was carried out to understand the formation of the stable layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material in an air atmosphere, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. A weight loss of 31% between 99 and 213 °C was depicted with a slope profile, which is attributed to the sluggish evaporation and removal of surface adsorbed water and dehydration of citric acid and cobalt nitrate precursor. A second weight loss of 35% between 213 and 297 °C was related to the simultaneous oxidation–decomposition of chelating agents, residual carbon of citric acid, and nitrate precursors. It can be seen that after 300 °C the TGA curve was observed as a straight line, which reveals that no weight loss occurred with the residual 32–36% weight at 750 °C, confirming the formation of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material in an air atmosphere, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. Moreover, the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material was quite stable up to 900 °C due to no weight loss occuring in the TGA curve, as indicated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. From the TGA results, the collected gel precursor from the sol–gel synthesis was calcined at 750 °C for 28 h, yielded the stable layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material, and then immediately stored in an argon-filled glovebox to prevent moisture contamination.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/medium/am1c04482_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Structural confirmation of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material: (a) Thermogravimetric curve for the gel precursor of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material, recorded from room temperature to 900 °C at a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> under an air atmosphere. (b) Powder X-ray diffraction pattern of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material (calcined at 750 °C for 28 h) with Rietveld refinement analysis. Stacking arrangement of P2-type structure built by (c) hexagonal oxygen arrangement (red) with a cobalt atom (cyan) located at the center. (d) Three layer CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedra sheets were represented in AB|BA|AB close-packed arrangement with the sodium atom (gray) between two CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedra layers. (e) Schematic illustration of P2-type structure with MO<sub>6</sub> (octahedra) and NaO<sub>6</sub> (trigonal prisms) for the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c04482&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern (asterisk symbol in black) and its Rietveld refinement analysis of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material (750 °C for 28 h) are depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b. All the diffraction peaks were well-indexed with the Bragg reflection (vertical pink line) related to the P2-type structure of the Na<sub>0.7</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> phase (JCPDS # 30-1182). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> The calculated lattice parameter values <i>a</i> = 2.8389 Å, <i>c</i> = 10.9899 Å and unit cell volume <i>V</i> = 76.71 Å<sup>3</sup> showed good agreement with the layered P2-type cathode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57 ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57 ref59" aria-label="reference 57 and 59">(57,59)</a> The goodness of fit (χ<sup>2</sup> = 1.5) and <i>R</i>-factor (<i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 1.4%) were obtained by Rietveld refinement analysis, and the crystallographic atomic parameters are given in Table S1 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/suppl_file/am1c04482_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). The calculated pattern (red) was completely fitted with the experimental data and observed reduced difference (blue), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b. The stacking arrangement of the P2-type structure is demonstrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c–e. The CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedra sheet was revealed a hexagonal oxygen arrangement (red) with a cobalt atom (cyan) located at the center, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c. The schematized pattern is exactly matched with the P2-type structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref20 ref46 ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref20 ref46 ref60" aria-label="reference 15, 20, 46 and 60">(15,20,46,60)</a> Then, three layer CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedra sheets were represented in AB|BA|AB close-packed arrangement with the sodium atom (gray) between two CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedra layers, as given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d. The complete schematic illustration of the P2-type structure is depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e, which disclosed that the layered structure was composed of two layers such as an octahedra CoO<sub>6</sub> layer and a trigonal prism Na layer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref20 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref20 ref46" aria-label="reference 15, 20 and 46">(15,20,46)</a> Further, the structural characteristic of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material was confirmed by Raman spectroscopy measurement, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/suppl_file/am1c04482_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. From this result, the presence of five Raman active modes E<sub>1g</sub>(O), E<sub>2g</sub>(O), 2E<sub>2g</sub>(Na), and A<sub>1g</sub>(O) was associated with the local atomic arrangements of the layered NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61 ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 61 and 62">(61,62)</a> The observed positions of Raman active vibration were well-coordinated with the hexagonal structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61 ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 61 and 62">(61,62)</a> Furthermore, the stoichiometric proportion of Na and Co elements in the synthesized P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material was confirmed with a 1:1 ratio (atomic percentage) by inductively coupled plasma–optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES) analysis.</div><div class="NLM_p">The morphology of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material was examined by field emission scanning electron microscopic (FESEM) analyses, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. The FESEM images of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a) revealed a flake sheet-like morphology, presented by different alignments. The flaky sheets revealed a hexagonal shape, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b,c. Significantly, the flaky sheets were stacked together and exhibited a flaky bundle morphology (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b,c). Furthermore, the single hexagonal flaky sheet was presented with a size of ∼300 nm (thickness), and the solid crystalline surface was determined (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a–c). The obtained hexagonal shape morphology was corroborated with the literature reports, representing layered structure characteristics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63" aria-label="reference 63">(63)</a> The simultaneous FESEM elemental mapping analyses of respective quantified area (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d,e) confirmed the presence of sodium (Na, cyan, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f), cobalt (Co, pink, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>g), and oxygen (O, green, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>h) elements in the synthesized layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material. The FESEM elemental mapping results indicate a strong signal to all the elements, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f–h. Importantly, the spectrum of the energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) analysis of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material showed a pronounced signal for the presence of sodium, cobalt, and oxygen elements without any other elements (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/suppl_file/am1c04482_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). From the obtained quantitative EDX analysis, the atomic percentages of sodium (26.9%), cobalt (21.2%), and oxygen (51.9%) in the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material were almost closer to the proposed composition of the P2-type layered NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/medium/am1c04482_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Morphological investigation of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material: FESEM images of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material shown as (a) flaky sheets morphology and located by different alignments, (b) flaky sheets revealed with a hexagonal layered shape, and (c) flaky sheets stacked together and exhibited a flaky bundle morphology with a size of ∼300 nm (thickness); (d) FESEM electron image for the simultaneous mapping analyses of the respective quantified area; (e) simultaneous elemental mapping image, which confirmed the presence of (f) sodium (Na, cyan), (g) cobalt (Co, pink), and (h) oxygen (O, green) elements in the synthesized layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c04482&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> High-Rate Layered P2-Type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</h3><div class="NLM_p">The sodium ion intercalation–deintercalation characteristics of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material were examined by a cyclic voltammetric (CV) technique between 2 and 4 V at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a. During the first cycle, the observed potential for the anodic oxidation peak at 3.08 V and cathodic reduction peaks at 3.03 and 2.79 V were different than those of the following subsequent CV cycles. Particularly, the oxidation peak at 3.08 V and reduction peaks at 3.03, 2.79 V (1st cycle) were shifted toward the left side (3.0 V) and right side (3.2 and 2.9 V) during the second cycle, as indicated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a. Moreover, the second cycle onward the CV profiles revealed nine redox peaks at 2.52/2.15, 2.60/2.28, 2.65/2.37, 2.71/2.44, 3.00/2.90, 3.17/3.12, 3.30/3.20, 3.69/3.61, and 3.99/3.93 V, associated with the same current densities, shapes, and peak positions during anodic and cathodic sweep. The presence of multiple redox peaks indicated the formation of different intermediate chemical compositions through a complex multiphase transition mechanism during the sodium-ion intercalation–deintercalation process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59 ref63 ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59 ref63 ref64" aria-label="reference 59, 63 and 64">(59,63,64)</a> From the obtained stable CV profiles, the multiphase transition mechanisms were confirmed to the reversible process of Na<sup>+</sup> ion intercalation–deintercalation for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59 ref63 ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59 ref63 ref64" aria-label="reference 59, 63 and 64">(59,63,64)</a> It can be seen that the complex multiphase transition mechanism of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material is more highly reversible than the literature reports <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59 ref63 ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59 ref63 ref64" aria-label="reference 59, 63 and 64">(59,63,64)</a> and reveals the tendency of a high-rate capability for SIBs.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/medium/am1c04482_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical characterization of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material: (a) sodium ion intercalation–deintercalation characteristics of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material examined by cyclic voltammetry technique between 2 and 4 V at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; voltage versus capacity profiles of sodium cells at (b) 0.5 C and (c) 1 C; (d) charge–discharge cycling studies of sodium cells for 1–100 cycles at 0.5 and 1 C; rate capability studies of (e) voltage versus capacity profiles and (f) capacity versus cycle number performance at different rates (0.1–5 C) in the voltage range 2–4 V using 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte in 1:1 EC–PC solvents.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c04482&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Thus, the Galvanostatic charge–discharge cycling studies were performed for the fabricated sodium cells (Na versus layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material) in the voltage range 2–4 V at 0.5 and 1 C (theoretical capacity 235 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) for 1–100 cycles, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b–d. The sodium cells yielded initial cycle charge–discharge capacities of 72/93 and 65/82 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 and 1 C rates. The obtained initial cycle charge capacities were less than the discharge capacities with significant changes in the voltage versus capacity profile (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b,c), related to the charging process that started at the open circuit potential (OCV). During the charging process, the oxidation process of Co<sup>3+</sup> to Co<sup>4+</sup> and deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions from the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material occurred, while discharging the process was completely reversible with the reduction of Co<sup>4+</sup> to Co<sup>3+</sup> and allowed for repeated charge–discharge cycles. After the first cycle, the charge–discharge capacities of 99/95 and 84/83 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (2nd cycle) at 0.5 and 1 C rates were almost closer and revealed stable charge–discharge voltage profiles, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b,c. The multiple flat voltage plateaus were associated with a multiphase transition mechanism during the Na<sup>+</sup> ion intercalation–deintercalation process <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref59" aria-label="reference 46 and 59">(46,59)</a> and corroborated to the multiple obtained redox peaks, as shown in the CV results (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a). Also, the pronounced discharge flat voltage plateau at 3.1 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b,c) corresponds to the CV reduction peak at 3.1 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a). Particularly, the charge–discharge voltage profiles at 1 C were quite stable without any significant changes during the cycles, as defined in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c. Then, the charge–discharge cycling studies of sodium cells (Na versus layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material) for 1–100 cycles delivered stable charge–discharge capacities of 98/96 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (5th cycle) and 81/81 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (100th cycle) at 0.5 C and 85/85 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (5th cycle) and 85/84 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (100th cycle) at 1 C, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d. The capacity retention between the first cycle to the 100th cycle and Coulombic efficiency were almost 100% at a 1 C rate, related to the sodium cell. The obtained unique cycling stabilities at 1 C are higher than the literature reports related to the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> Furthermore, charge–discharge cycling studies of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material were extended to the rate capability test at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, and 5 C, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e,f. From the plots of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e, it was observed that there were no significant changes in the voltage profiles at different rates (0.1–5 C). Then, the maximum applied charge–discharge current was 3.3 mA (5 C rate, active material weight ∼2.8 mg) for CR-2032 sodium coin cell, which is a higher applied current for the sodium cell than the normal charge–discharge cycling studies at around 0.1 mA. The obtained stable discharge capacities of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material at different current densities were 103 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (0.1 C), 99 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (0.2 C), 95 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (0.5 C), 90 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (1 C), 82 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (2 C), 78 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (3 C), and 71 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (5 C), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f. The charge–discharge capacity drop during the rate studies of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material was less and showed a remarkable capacity retention of 99 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a 0.1 C rate (70–80 cycles, after tested at 5 C rate).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> <i>In Situ</i> Thermal Safety Aspects of NaCoO<sub>2</sub> Cathode Using Multimode Calorimetry Analysis</h3><div class="NLM_p">After confirmation of the structural characterizations and the charge–discharge cycling studies, the thermodynamic instability of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material in sodium cells was examined by the multimode calorimetric technique. In this study, the heat flux of sodium cells is exactly measured with various battery chemistries related to the thermal runaway mechanism. Thus, the thermal safety characteristics of sodium cells (contains the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material, sodium metal, 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte in EC–PC solvents, glass fiber separator, and multiple CR-2032 coin cell components) were obtained by the <i>in situ</i> process during the thermally abuse test from 30 to 300 °C. The sodium cell at a charged/discharged state was placed in the sample cell holder, while the sealed empty CR-2032 coin cell was connected in the reference coin cell holder. The reference and sample coin cell holders were connected with heat flux and temperature sensors and placed into the heating chamber to perform a thermally abuse test. The whole heating chamber was surrounded by a high-temperature heater to perform different types of experiments such as isothermal cycling and calorimetry. Hence, the characteristics of thermal runaway, short-circuit, separator melting, electrode decomposition reaction, gas generation, exothermic reaction, released heat energy, and gasket melting could be determined by a MMC technique.</div><div class="NLM_p">The sodium cell was cycled at 0.1 C for three cycles and limited at the fourth cycle charge state (4 V) with a charge capacity of 97 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a), connected in the MMC sample coin cell holder and positioned into the heating chamber. Then, the sodium cell at a charged state was heated from 30 to 300 °C at a heating rate of 0.5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>, and the heat flux and the cell voltage were measured, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b. In the charged MMC data (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, red), a profound endothermic peak at around 100 °C was observed for sodium metal melting, and then, an immediate exothermic reaction with a released heat energy of 36 J g<sup>–1</sup> was shown (indicated in orange), while the cell voltage suddenly decreased to 0.5 V (green, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b) related to the heating time at around 2 h, as shown in time scale. Then, an exothermic reaction at 147 °C was obtained with a higher released heat energy of 62 J g<sup>–1</sup> (indicated in purple), which can be associated with the charged energy for the sodium cell, created by the chemical potential difference between sodium metal and the highly oxidized desodiated layered P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material. At the same time, the cell voltage was measured at 1.2 V due to pressure buildup, and then, the cell voltage suddenly dropped to 0 V, which indicates an internal short circuit and a thermal runaway event. It could have occurred due to the direct reaction between the liquid phase sodium metal and the highly oxidized desodiated layered P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material at above 147 °C. Further, the next exothermic peak at 207 °C with a released heat energy of 145 J g<sup>–1</sup> (indicated in brown) was associated with the reaction between the highly oxidized desodiated layered P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material and the presence of an organic electrolyte (1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> salt in EC–PC solvents). Significantly, the exothermic peak between 230 and 290 °C with a released heat energy of 307 J g<sup>–1</sup> was attributed to the decomposition reaction of the desodiated layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material, corroborated with the decomposition reaction of the cathode material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref65'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65" aria-label="reference 65">(65)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/medium/am1c04482_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. <i>In situ</i> thermal safety characteristics of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material: (a) voltage versus capacity profiles of charge–discharge cycling studies between 2 and 4 V at 0.1 C; <i>in situ</i> MMC results for the sodium cell (b) charge state at 4 V, (c) discharge state at 2.8 V, and (d) discharge state at 2 V performed from 30 to 300 °C at a heating rate of 0.5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>, and the heat flux and the cell voltage relating to temperature and time were measured. The sodium cells were composed of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material, sodium metal, 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte in EC–PC solvents, glass fiber separator, and multiple CR-2032 coin cell components.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c04482&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Subsequently, the MMC studies for the discharged sodium cell were performed from 30 to 300 °C at the same heating rate of 0.5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> and the heat flux (red) and the cell voltage (green) related to temperature were measured, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c,d. From the MMC data, the discharged sodium cell at 2.8 and 2 V with discharge capacities of at around 60 and 98 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 C for the fourth cycle showed a similar profound endothermic peak at 100 °C for sodium metal melting, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c,d. Significantly, the discharge state sodium cell at 2.8 V did not show a considerable exothermic peak between 125 and 230 °C, which reveals a less organic electrolyte reaction. Nevertheless, the predominant exothermic peak appeared at 286 °C with the highest released heat energy of 1286 J g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c, indicated in cyan) for the cathode decomposition reaction.
