Also, the discharge state of the sodium cell at 2 V revealed significant exothermic peaks at 212 °C (168 J g<sup>–1</sup>, maroon), 255 °C (151 J g<sup>–1</sup>, pink), and 284 °C (410 J g<sup>–1</sup>, cyan), associated with the organic electrolyte and the cathode reaction, followed by the thermal decomposition of cathode reaction with a leased total heat energy of 740 J g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d) rather than the charged sodium cell, which leased a total heat energy of 550 J g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b). The cell voltage of discharged sodium cell was stable from 30 to 99 °C and suddenly dropped to 1 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c,d, green) due to sodium melting at 100 °C and revealed voltage fluctuation, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b (green). Then, the pronounced exothermic peak at 212 °C with a released heat energy of 168 J g<sup>–1</sup> was associated with the reaction of the sodiated layered P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material with 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte in EC–PC solvents. The prime exothermic peak at 284 °C with a released heat energy of 410 J g<sup>–1</sup> showed the decomposition reaction of the sodiated layered P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material. Thus, the released heat energies of 1359 J g<sup>–1</sup> (discharge state at 2.8 V) and 740 J g<sup>–1</sup> (discharge state at 2 V) were higher than the charge state heat energy of 550 J g<sup>–1</sup>. To investigate the failure analysis, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref66'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref66" aria-label="reference 66">(66)</a> after the MMC studies were performed (30–300 °C), the MMC tested sodium cells were disassembled and it was observed that the glass fiber separator of the charge state sodium cell did not show any significant change compared to the discharge state glass fiber separator, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/suppl_file/am1c04482_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> <i>Ex Situ</i> Thermal Safety Studies of NaCoO<sub>2</sub> Using Differential Scanning Calorimetry Analysis</h3><div class="NLM_p">To determine the exothermic peak onset temperature, the thermal safety characteristics of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material at a charge and discharge status were examined using DSC analysis at an elevated temperature (50–325 °C) for SIBs, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>. For more than four decades, the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material has been studied as a promising cathode for SIBs, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 32 to 36">(32−36)</a> while the thermal safety aspects are not clear due to highly reactive sodium metal in the presence of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte, which contains a mixture of ethylene carbonate and propylene carbonate (EC–PC) in 1:1 (v/v ratio). Hence, the sodium cell (Na versus NaCoO<sub>2</sub>) cycling was limited at the fourth cycle charge state at 2.8, 3.5, and 4 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, #1) and discharge state at 3, 2.5, and 2 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b, #2) with the different state of charge (40, 70, and 105 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and discharge capacities (45, 60, and 97 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). The DSC measurement was performed on the collected layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material (∼0.5 mg), which was placed into a DSC pan in the presence of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte (1:1 ratio, ∼0.5 μL). The DSC pan was sealed with a lid in an argon-filled glovebox (O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O level monitored at &lt;0.5 ppm) and was kept on a DSC pan stage along with the reference DSC pan (sealed empty DSC pan).</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/medium/am1c04482_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. <i>Ex situ</i> thermal safety characteristics of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material: DSC spectra of (a) charge state at 2.8, 3.5, and 4 V and (b) discharge state at 3, 2.5, and 2 V for the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material, represented with the heat flow versus temperature at an elevated temperature (50–325 °C). The insets #1 and #2 reveal the voltage versus capacity profiles of charge–discharge cycling studies between 2 and 4 V at 0.1 C to determine different states of charge and discharge conditions.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c04482&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The obtained DSC spectra of the charge state (2.8 V, red; 3.5 V, green; 4 V, maroon) and discharge state (3 V, red; 2.5 V, green; 2 V, maroon) of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material were represented with the heat flow versus temperature, as given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,b. The desodiated layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material at different charge states (2.8, 3.5, and 4 V) revealed two predominant exothermic peaks at around 220 °C (282, 360, and 312 J g<sup>–1</sup>) and 260 °C (156 and 150 J g<sup>–1</sup>), and the released total heat energy at around 438, 510, and 312 J g<sup>–1</sup> indicates thermodynamic instability (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). Significantly, the first predominant exothermic peak of charged state at 4 V started with an onset temperature 199 °C (denoted COT, pink) related to the reaction between the highly oxidized desodiated layered P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material and the presence of an organic electrolyte (1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte in EC–PC solvents). The second exothermic peak located at around 265 °C with less released heat energy at 156 J g<sup>–1</sup> (2.8 V) and 150 J g<sup>–1</sup> (3.5 V) were associated with the thermal decomposition reaction of the desodiated layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material. The thermal decomposition reaction can be related to the formation of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, CoO, Co, sodium-containing compounds, loss of oxygen, etc., as determined by accelerating rate calorimetry <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> and <i>in situ</i> transmission electron microscopic techniques. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> Significantly, the exothermic peaks between 160 and 280 °C were not observed in DSC spectra of NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolytes containing EC and PC solvents, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref55" aria-label="reference 19 and 55">(19,55)</a> which indicates the thermodynamic instability of the desodiated layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material with a NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte (EC–PC). Importantly, the desodiated layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material at a 4 V charge state released less total heat energy (312 J g<sup>–1</sup>) than the charge state at 2.8 V (438 J g<sup>–1</sup>) and 3.5 V (510 J g<sup>–1</sup>). Moreover, the DSC spectrum of the sodiated layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material at the discharge state (3 V, red; 2.5 V, green; 2 V, maroon) revealed two pronounced exothermic peaks at around 200 and 266 °C. Pointedly, the spectrum of the discharge state at 2 V disclosed the prime exothermic peak at 197 °C with a released higher total heat energy of 594 J g<sup>–1</sup> than the discharge state at 3.0 V (299 J g<sup>–1</sup>) and 2.5 V (369 J g<sup>–1</sup>). Significantly, the observed discharge first peak onset temperature at 141 °C (DOT, cyan) is less than the charge onset temperature at 199 °C (COT) and indicates it is highly reactive with electrolyte rather than in the charged condition. Notably, the decomposition reaction of the sodiated layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material at 266 °C released a heat energy of 156 J g<sup>–1</sup>, which indicates it is more unsafe than in the charged condition. The observed high thermal instability and higher released heat energy of the discharged state (594 J g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 V, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/suppl_file/am1c04482_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>) rather than the charged state (312 J g<sup>–1</sup> at 4 V, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/suppl_file/am1c04482_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>) can be attributed to the presence of higher Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the discharged layered P2-type Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material than the pristine cathode material, associated with the delivered higher discharge capacity of 97 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> than the initial charge capacity of 78 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>, #1 and #2). Pointedly, the DSC spectra of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material revealed the thermodynamic instability at above 160 °C, which makes the whole battery system unsafe for high-power applications. Thus, in the <i>in situ</i> MMC results, the obtained three exothermic peaks at around 207, 250, and 275 °C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b) were matched with the DSC exothermic peaks in the charged DSC spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). The released total heat energies of 594 J g<sup>–1</sup> (DSC discharge spectrum at 2 V) and 740 J g<sup>–1</sup> (MMC discharge cell at 2 V) for the discharge state were higher than those of the charge state at 4 V (DSC, 312 J g<sup>–1</sup> and MMC, 550 J g<sup>–1</sup>), which is unsafe. Significantly, it is pertinent to note that the spectrum of the discharge state at 2 V revealed and more decreased onset temperature (DOT) at 141 °C than the charge state onset temperature (COT) at 191 °C, which indicates it is more unsafe than the charge condition. Therefore, this study can be used to material design for high-safety SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref67'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref67" aria-label="reference 67">(67)</a></div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, a layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material was synthesized by the sol–gel method followed by heat treatment with a hexagonal shape flake sheet-like morphology with a size of ∼300 nm (thickness). In CV studies, multiple redox peaks were identified with the same current densities, shapes, and peak positions during the anodic and cathodic sweep, associated with the highly reversible phase transition reaction of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material. Thus, the sodium cells yielded stable charge–discharge capacities of 93/92 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 C and 87/87 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 C (50th cycle) for SIBs. Also, the rate studies showed remarkable stable performance and delivered discharge capacities of 103 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (0.1 C), 99 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (0.2 C), 95 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (0.5 C), 90 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (1 C), 82 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (2 C), 78 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (3 C), and 71 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (5 C). Then, the <i>in situ</i> thermal safety studies of MMC revealed that the thermal explosion event occurred by short-circuit, electrode decomposition reaction, gas generation, exothermic reaction, released heat energy, and gasket melting. Remarkably, the calculated charge state (at 4 V) and discharge state (at 2 V) released total heat energies of ∼550/740 J g<sup>–1</sup> for <i>in situ</i> MMC studies and ∼312/594 J g<sup>–1</sup> for <i>ex situ</i> DSC analyses, which revealed that the discharged state of the sodiated layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material is more unsafe than the charge state. The <i>ex situ</i> DSC spectra of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material at charge (4 V) and discharge states (2 V) revealed the pronounced two exothermic peaks at 225 °C (312 J g<sup>–1</sup>) and 197 °C (438 J g<sup>–1</sup>) corresponding to their released higher total heat energies of 312 and 594 J g<sup>–1</sup> (charge/discharge). The released total heat energies of 594 J g<sup>–1</sup> for the DSC discharged spectrum and 740 J g<sup>–1</sup> for the MMC discharged state were observed with higher exothermic peaks than with the charge condition (DSC, 312 J g<sup>–1</sup> and MMC, 550 J g<sup>–1</sup>) of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode, showing it is unsafe. Ultimately, it was confirmed by the decreased onset temperature (DOT) at 141 °C for the predominant exothermic peak at 197 °C than the charge state onset temperature (COT) at 191 °C for the observed exothermic peak at 225 °C; this indicates that it is more unsafe than the charged condition and can lead to new insights of thermal safety aspects for SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">4.  Experimental Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">4.1.  Synthesis of P2-Type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> Material</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The stoichiometric amount NaNO<sub>3</sub> (Merck) and Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>.6H<sub>2</sub>O (Merck) precursors were completely dissolved in 20 mL of Millipore water and stirred for 3 h. The chelating agent of the citric acid monohydrate (Alfa Aesar) solution was added to the metal nitrate precursor and concentrated at 80 °C for 6 h. An excess amount of 20% NaNO<sub>3</sub> precursor was used in the metal precursor solution to compensate for the sodium-ion loss during high-temperature calcination. At the end of the concentration and evaporation steps, the gel precursor was obtained and dried at 120 °C for 24 h. Then, the gel precursor was calcined at 750 °C for 28 h and yielded a crystalline layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material. Importantly, during the cooling process, at around 150 °C, the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material was immediately transferred to a glovebox (filled with argon, and H<sub>2</sub>O/O<sub>2</sub> levels were &lt;0.5 ppm) to prevent atmospheric moisture. Finally, the powder was well-ground for 10 min using a mortar and pestle in an argon atmosphere and used for further thermal safety studies of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">4.2.  Physicochemical Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was carried out to determine the decomposition temperature of the gel precursor and the formation of the stable layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material at a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> from room temperature to 900 °C in the air using a TGA instrument model (Netzsch Tarsus 209). The crystallinity and phase purity of the synthesized layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material were examined by powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern using a D2-phaser, Bruker, 30 kV/10 mA machine in the 2θ range 10°–80°. Then, the lattice parameters were calculated by Rietveld refinement analysis using GSAS software. For the further confirmation of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material, Raman spectroscopy measurements were performed by a Horiba Jobin Yvon instrument with a 514 nm green laser as the excitation light source. Then, the stoichiometric proportion of the synthesized P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material was examined using inductively coupled plasma–optical emission spectrometry (ICAP 7400 from Thermo Fisher Scientific) to quantify the presence of Na and Co elements. The morphology, particle size, elemental mapping, and energy dispersive X-ray analysis of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material were examined by a field emission scanning electron microscopic (FESEM) instrument (Nova NanoSEM 200).</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">4.3.  Electrochemical Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical properties of the synthesized layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material were examined by galvanostatic charge–discharge studies using fabricated CR-2032 sodium cells. The sodium cells were assembled using layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode versus sodium metal with 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte, which contains an ethylene carbonate–propylene carbonate (EC–PC) mixture in a 1:1 (v/v) ratio and a glass fiber separator. The layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode was fabricated by doctor-blade coating of the slurry on aluminum foil using a MTI laminate coater. A Thinky planetary mixer was used to prepare the homogenized cathode slurry, containing 80% layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode active material, 10% super carbon, and 10% poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVdF) dissolved in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP). After slurry coating, the cathode was dried at 80 °C for 12 h in a vacuum oven and cut at a size of 14 mm diameter related to an average loading mass of active material at ∼2.8 mg. All the sodium cells were assembled in a glovebox (NEXUS II Vacuum Atmospheres Co.), filled with argon; the moisture levels were controlled at less than 0.5 ppm. To determine the Na<sup>+</sup> ion intercalation–deintercalation characteristics and redox reaction in the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode, cyclic voltammogram (CV) measurements were examined for the sodium-ion cells using a Gamry instrument, recorded at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage range 2–4 V for 1–5 cycles. The Galvanostatic charge–discharge cycling and high-rate capability studies of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material were investigated using an Arbin cycler (Model BT2043) at 25 °C in the voltage range 2–4 V at the different current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, and 5 C.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">4.4.  Thermal Safety Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The multimode calorimetry technique (MMC, 274 Nexus, Netzsch Gerätebau GmbH) was used for the <i>in situ</i> thermal safety characteristics study of the actual sodium coin cell (CR-2032), containing the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material, sodium metal, 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte in EC–PC solvents, glass fiber separator, and multiple CR-2032 coin cell components. In this technique, the sodium cells were thermally abused from 30 to 300 °C at a heating rate of 0.5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>, while the assembled empty CR-2032 coin cell was used as a reference cell and connected in the coin cell holder. Thus, the thermal runaway event of sodium cells was identified by the MMC technique related to the characteristics of the thermodynamic instability of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material, short-circuit, electrode decomposition reaction, and gasket melting. Significantly, the <i>ex situ</i> thermal properties of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material were carried out by a differential scanning calorimetry technique (DSC, TA Instruments Q20 unit) at an elevated temperature (50–325 °C) with the rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. To perform an <i>ex situ</i> DSC analysis, the desodiated/sodiated layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material was collected from the cycled sodium cell and placed (∼0.5 mg) into an aluminum hermetic pan in the presence of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte (0.5 μL, 1:1 ratio). Then, the DSC pan was sealed with a lid in an argon-filled glovebox (O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O level monitored at &lt;0.5 ppm) and used for DSC measurement, while an empty DSC pan was used as a reference. The DSC spectrum was collected with the heat flow versus temperature, and the released heat energy was calculated on the basis of the onset temperature.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c04482?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c04482</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Figures of Raman spectrum, EDX analysis, disassembled sodium cells photographs, and DSC spectra and table of crystallographic atomic parameters (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/suppl_file/am1c04482_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/suppl_file/am1c04482_si_001.pdf">am1c04482_si_001.pdf (679.35 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c04482" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Researchers must find a sustainable way of providing the power our modern lifestyles demand.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrxUs7wptoM7LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhWvkcdjpY9cw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1cXhs1Kntrc%253D&amp;md5=75c8cd94c450a1682aa6d1bd9bfe1c1f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202003668"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palanisamy, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Parekh, M. H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pol, V. G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">In Situ Replenishment of Formation Cycle Lithium-Ion Loss for Enhancing Battery Life</span>. <i>Adv. Funct. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>30</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">46</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">2003668</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adfm.202003668</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D30%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D2003668%26journal%3DAdv.%2BFunct.%2BMater.%26issue%3D46%26author%3DM.%2BPalanisamy%26author%3DM.%2BH.%2BParekh%26author%3DV.%2BG.%2BPol%26title%3DIn%2BSitu%2BReplenishment%2Bof%2BFormation%2BCycle%2BLithium-Ion%2BLoss%2Bfor%2BEnhancing%2BBattery%2BLife%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadfm.202003668&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c04482&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadfm.202003668&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In Situ Replenishment of Formation Cycle Lithium-Ion Loss for Enhancing Battery Life</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palanisamy, Manikandan; Parekh, Mihit H.; Pol, Vilas G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">30</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">46</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2003668</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In situ replenishment of formation cycle lithium-ion loss is considered for the development of longer-lasting rechargeable batteries, contg. a thin lithium reservoir-electrode to mitigate the formation cycle capacity loss.  Synchronized lithium and lithium-ion batteries (SLLIB) deliver specific charge-discharge capacities of 147/145 mAh g-1 for mesocarbon microbeads (MCMB) vs. LiFePO4 and 186/171 mAh g-1 for C-Si vs. LiNi1/3Mn1/3Co1/3O2 at 0.2 C.  The energy-reduced cells (due to solid electrolyte interface (SEI) formation) are replenished and achieved an increased energy d. of 455 Wh kg-1 with retained flat-voltage profile for SLLIB-MCMB vs. LiFePO4 and 576 Wh kg-1 for SLLIB-C-Si vs. LiNi1/3Mn1/3Co1/3O2 through Li+ ion in situ reservoir replenishment from the reservoir-electrode to the MCMB/C-Si anode.  Furthermore, reserve mode cycling process delivers charge-discharge capacities of 126/124 mAh g-1 for SLLIB-Li vs. LiFePO4 and 137/136 mAh g-1 for SLLIB-Li vs. LiNi1/3Mn1/3Co1/3O2 at 0.2 C, assocd. with the Li+ ion transportation between reservoir-electrode and LiFePO4/LiNi1/3Mn1/3Co1/3O2 cathode, confirmed by the Li+ ion diffusion path through MCMB and C-Si electrodes, using EIS anal.  Ultimately, the in situ reservoir replenishment, replenished cycling and reserve mode cycling processes of SLLIB leads to enhanced battery life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpxZuRQWLsAULVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhWvkcdjpY9cw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhvVajsbfE&amp;md5=a08c2e0d91684e5896a4856774cecb85</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1039/c3ta13621c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manikandan, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Periasamy, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jagannathan, R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sol-Gel Synthesis and Impedance Characteristics of Networked Nanocrystalline Olivine Cathode for Li-Ion Full Cells</span>. <i>J. Mater. Chem. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">48</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">15397</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">15405</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sol-gel synthesis and impedance characteristics of networked nanocrystalline olivine cathode for Li-ion full cells</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Manikandan, P.; Periasamy, P.; Jagannathan, R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A sol-gel synthesis using adipic acid yielded small particles of ∼20 nm in size of olivine LiFePO4/C cathode materials.  In the characterization of cathode system(s), solid state impedance spectra of the pristine LiFePO4/C cathode revealed clear localization of charge through charge build-up.  When networked with MWCNT, this material facilitates enhancement in charge mobility, eventually explaining the capacity enhancement of the LiFePO4/C-MWCNT electrode, which yields a high capacity of 163 mA-h/g at C/10.  On the other hand, the lower capacity of 125 mA-h/g found for the pristine LiFePO4/C electrode material can be explained in terms of charge becoming localized/trapped in the vicinity of inter- and intra-granular regions of the cathode particles.  To get a broader view of the application potential (in terms of cell voltages of ∼3 V, 2 V, and safety aspects) of the networked cathode materials, two kinds of Li-ion full cells using mesocarbon microbeads and Li4Ti5O12 as anodes were fabricated, which yielded capacities of 1.94 and 2.1 mA-h, resp.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo0g3Q9zpxk67Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhWvkcdjpY9cw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhvVWmsb3O&amp;md5=5a623debb4d3f324828b7af879d4c497</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C4RA09400J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manikandan, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Periasamy, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jagannathan, R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Nano-Granular Sn Impregnated NiTi Alloy Matrix Anode for High Voltage Li-Ion Pouch Cells</span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">5</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">3844</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3853</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rechargeable Seawater Battery and Its Electrochemical Mechanism</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Jae-Kwang; Lee, Eungje; Kim, Hyojin; Johnson, Christopher; Cho, Jaephil; Kim, Youngsik</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemElectroChem</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Herein, we explore the electrochem. mechanism of a novel rechargeable seawater battery system that uses seawater as the cathode material.  Sodium is harvested from seawater while charging the battery, and the harvested sodium is discharged with oxygen dissolved in the seawater, functioning as oxidants to produce electricity.  The seawater provides both anode (Na metal) and cathode (O2) materials for the proposed battery.  Based on the discharge voltage (∼2.9 V) with participation of O2 and the charge voltage (∼4.1 V) with Cl2 evolution during the first cycle, a voltage efficiency of about 73 % is obtained.  If the seawater battery is constructed using hard carbon as the anode and a Na super ion conductor as the solid electrolyte, a strong cycle performance of 84 % is obsd. after 40 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo8m2Tz-bgctbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhxDs7etlUT1A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXotlKitr4%253D&amp;md5=7022ee7000199c5c1f7e081822f23d7e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1016/S0016-0032(20)90061-3"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Washington, H. S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Chemistry of the Earth’s Crust</span>. <i>J. Franklin Inst.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1920</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>190</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">6</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">757</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">815</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/S0016-0032(20)90061-3</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D190%26publication_year%3D1920%26pages%3D757-815%26journal%3DJ.%2BFranklin%2BInst.%26issue%3D6%26author%3DH.%2BS.%2BWashington%26title%3DThe%2BChemistry%2Bof%2Bthe%2BEarth%25E2%2580%2599s%2BCrust%26doi%3D10.1016%252FS0016-0032%252820%252990061-3&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c04482&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2FS0016-0032%2820%2990061-3&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Chemistry of the earth's crust</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Washington, Henry S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Franklin Institute</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1920</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">190</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">757-815</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JFINAB</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0016-0032</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">The number of essential rock-forming minerals is very small, and includes chiefly silicates of Al, Fe, Mg, Ca, Na, and K.  The av. igneous rock is approx. a granodiorite.  The 12 elements, O, Si, Al, Fe, Ca, Na, K, Mg, Ti, H, P, and Mn form 99.61% of the earth's crust.  The chem. elements fall into 2 groups, petrogenic and metallogenic.  The petrogenic elements are characteristic of and most abundant in the igneous rocks, have a low at. wt., and normally occur as oxides, silicates, chlorides, and fluorides.  The metallogenic elements are rare or absent in the igneous rocks, have a high at. wt., occur as ores (native metals, sulfides, arsenides, bromides, etc., but not primarily oxides or silicates).  It is suggested that beneath the silicate crust of petrogenic elements occurs first a zone essentially of Ni and Fe, then a central core of metallogenic elements.  In igneous rocks and minerals, the elements show a correlation in that certain of them are prone to occur with others.  Igneous rocks are distributed in comagmatic regions, i. e., regions of chem. related magmas.  Calcn. is made of the rock ds. from their chem. compn., also of the an. chem. compn. and ds. of the continental masses and oceanic floors.  The results show that the an. ds. of the continents, ocean floors, and various smaller regions of the earth stand in inverse relation to their elevations; these facts are confirmative of the theory of isostasy.  Attention is paid to the general characters of igneous rocks, the presence of gases and water vapor in the magma, and the analogy of the latter with a salt soln.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqQJSXxtxSHi7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhxDs7etlUT1A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaB3MXitVSg&amp;md5=b28b823e5c870878d2e6153a34df8589</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1016/j.coche.2015.08.007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dysart, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A comprehensive study is reported entailing optimization of sodium ion electrolyte formulation and compatibility studies with pos. and neg. electrode materials.  EC:PC:DMC and EC:PC:DME were found to exhibit optimum ionic conductivities and lower viscosities.  Yet, hard carbon neg. electrode materials tested in such electrolytes exhibit significant differences in performance, rooted in the different resistivity of the SEI, which results in too large polarization and concomitant loss of capacity at low potentials when DME is used as a co-solvent.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Slater, Michael D.; Kim, Donghan; Lee, Eungje; Doeff, Marca; Johnson, Christopher S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojsFXLPEQzILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0limVY9XVylC4Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XntlSnurc%253D&amp;md5=fa5d7d089d261a40896cb6b03e88bfbe</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40811f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, S.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Charge carriers in rechargeable batteries: Na ions vs. Li ions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong, Sung You; Kim, Youngjin; Park, Yuwon; Choi, Aram; Choi, Nam-Soon; Lee, Kyu Tae</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  We discuss the similarities and dissimilarities of sodium- and lithium-ion batteries in terms of neg. and pos. electrodes.  Compared to the comprehensive body of work on lithium-ion batteries, research on sodium-ion batteries is still at the germination stage.  Since both sodium and lithium are alkali metals, they share similar chem. properties including ionicity, electronegativity and electrochem. reactivity.  They accordingly have comparable synthetic protocols and electrochem. performances, which indicates that sodium-ion batteries can be successfully developed based on previously applied approaches or methods in the lithium counterpart.  The electrode materials in Li-ion batteries provide the best library for research on Na-ion batteries because many Na-ion insertion hosts have their roots in Li-ion insertion hosts.  However, the larger size and different bonding characteristics of sodium ions influence the thermodn. and/or kinetic properties of sodium-ion batteries, which leads to unexpected behavior in electrochem. performance and reaction mechanism, compared to lithium-ion batteries.  This perspective provides a comparative overview of the major developments in the area of pos. and neg. electrode materials in both Li-ion and Na-ion batteries in the past decade.  Highlighted are concepts in solid state chem. and electrochem. that have provided new opportunities for tailored design that can be extended to many different electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqHBS0wIkomorVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0limVY9XVylC4Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtVWmtL%252FE&amp;md5=023136cc0e5ed94e18ce0d526a0000a4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40847g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Room-temperature stationary sodium-ion batteries for large-scale electric energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Huilin; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging.  However, after intensive research efforts, we believe that low-cost, long-life and room-temp. sodium-ion batteries would be promising for applications in large-scale energy storage system in the near future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpqNKJTie6WmbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0limVY9XVylC4Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtFOisrzJ&amp;md5=2e02460553edc21e1ea8b73982ed63bb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Revealing the Intercalation Mechanisms of Lithium, Sodium, and Potassium in Hard Carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Alvin, Stevanus; Cahyadi, Handi Setiadi; Hwang, Jieun; Chang, Wonyoung; Kwak, Sang Kyu; Kim, Jaehoon</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2000283</span>CODEN:

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon is the most promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries and potassium-ion batteries owing to its high stability, widespread availability, low-cost, and excellent performance.  Understanding the carrier-ion storage mechanism is a prerequisite for developing high-performance electrode materials; however, the underlying ion storage mechanism in hard carbon has been a topic of debate because of its complex structure.  Herein, it is demonstrated that the Li+-, Na+-, and K+-ion storage mechanisms in hard carbon are based on the adsorption of ions on the surface of active sites (e.g., deffects, edges, and residual heteroatoms) in the sloping voltage region, followed by intercalation into the graphitic layers in the low-voltage plateau region.  At a low c.d. of 3 mA g-1, the graphitic layers of hard carbon are unlocked to permit Li+-ion intercalation, resulting in a plateau region in the lithium-ion batteries.  To gain insights into the ion storage mechanism, exptl. observations including various ex situ techniques, a const.-current const.-voltage method, and diffusivity measurements are correlated with the theor. estn. of changes in carbon structures and insertion voltages during ion insertion obtained using the d. functional theory.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo3nciM-7eFM7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0limVY9XVylC4Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXntVOqsr4%253D&amp;md5=4a410f22779904b797847b465d1c4a5b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1039/C9CS00162J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Adelhelm, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Titirici, M.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Intercalation chemistry of graphite and alkali metal ions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Yuqi; Lu, Yaxiang; Adelhelm, Philipp; Titirici, Maria-Magdalena; Hu, Yong-Sheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">48</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4655-4687</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Reversibly intercalating ions into host materials for electrochem. energy storage is the essence of the working principle of rocking-chair type batteries.  The most relevant example is the graphite anode for rechargeable Li-ion batteries which has been commercialized in 1991 and still represents the benchmark anode in Li-ion batteries 30 years later.  Learning from past lessons on alkali metal intercalation in graphite, recent breakthroughs in sodium and potassium intercalation in graphite have been demonstrated for Na-ion batteries and K-ion batteries.  Interestingly, some significant differences proved to exist for the intercalation of Na+ and K+ into graphite compared with the Li+ case.  This review summarizes significant advances from both exptl. and theor. calcns. with a focus on comparing the intercalation of three alkali metal ions (Li+, Na+, K+) into graphite and aims to clarify the intimate host-guest relationships and the underlying mechanisms.  New approaches developed to achieve favorable intercalation coupled with the challenges in this field are also discussed.  We also extrapolate alkali metal ion intercalation in graphite to mono-/multi-valent ions in layered electrode materials, which will deepen the understanding of intercalation chem. and provide guidance to explore new guests and hosts.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq6MFV28Yn2VrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg-LQUnvJ_u1w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhtlGgu7nO&amp;md5=7be03349c72a71fd63a908e09c05cd85</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2013.06.109"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chihara, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Okada, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamaki, J.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical and thermal properties of hard carbon-type anodes for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Jie; Zhao, Liwei; Chihara, Kuniko; Okada, Shigeto; Yamaki, Jun-ichi; Matsumoto, Shingo; Kuze, Satoru; Nakane, Kenji</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The hard carbon C1600 was studied as an anode material for Na-ion batteries.  The electrochem. performance of the C1600 electrode was investigated in four kinds of typical electrolytes: 1 mol/dm3 NaClO4/EC-DMC, 1 mol/dm3 NaClO4/PC, 1 mol/dm3 NaPF6/EC-DMC, and 1 mol/dm3 NaPF6/PC.  The C1600 electrodes showed excellent electrochem. properties in NaClO4-contg. electrolytes.  An initial Na insertion/extn. capacity of 413/321 mAh/g was obtained in the NaClO4/EC-DMC electrolyte, followed by a good capacity retention of 90% over 50 cycles.  These are the best electrochem. properties reported thus far for carbonaceous anode materials in Na-ion batteries.  The safety risk of C1600 anodes for Na-ion batteries was evaluated by using a TG-DSC system.  Based on the results of DSC anal. for simple electrolytes, the thermal behaviors of mixts. of the sodiated/lithiated C1600 and the assocd. electrolyte in a temp. range from room temp. to 400°C were investigated and compared.  The C1600 anodes showed better thermal stability in EC-DMC-based electrolytes than in PC-based ones.  Moreover, although Na metal is much more unstable than Li metal, the anode and electrolyte mixt. of Na-ion batteries showed similar or better thermal stability than that of Li-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoR9rIuynQOGbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lg-LQUnvJ_u1w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtV2ksrzM&amp;md5=7087b2b486e1f6fb92fae586da6d1e7b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1016/0167-2738(88)90351-7"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ge, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fouletier, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Intercalation of Sodium in Graphite</span>. <i>Solid State Ionics</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1988</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>28–30</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1172</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1175</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

    </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. behavior of graphite was studied by using the electrochem. cell: graphite|(polyethylene oxide, CF3SO3Na)|Na.         </div><div class="casAbstract">P2-phase Na0.74CoO2 cathode material prepd. by a solid-state method exhibits a sp. discharge capacity of 107 mA-h/g at 0.1C with good cycling performance for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">P2-type Nax[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2 made from earth-abundant elements for rechargeable Na batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Kajiyama, Masataka; Iwatate, Junichi; Nishikawa, Heisuke; Hitomi, Shuji; Okuyama, Ryoichi; Usui, Ryo; Yamada, Yasuhiro; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable Li batteries have risen to prominence as key devices for green and sustainable energy development.  Mn- and Fe-based cathode materials, such as LiMn2O4 and LiFePO4, are used in large-scale batteries for elec. vehicles.  Mn and Fe are abundant elements in the Earth's crust, but Li is not.  In contrast to Li, Na is an attractive charge carrier from elemental abundance.  Recently, some layered materials, where Na can be electrochem. and reversibly extd./inserted, have been reported.  However, their reversible capacity is typically limited to 100 mA-h/g.  Herein, the authors report a new electrode material, P2-Na2/3[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2, that delivers 190 mA-h/g of reversible capacity in Na cells with the electrochem. active Fe3+/Fe4+ redox.                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A multifunctional 3.5 V iron-based phosphate cathode for rechargeable batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ellis, B.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In the search for new pos.-electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries, recent research has focused on nanostructured lithium transition-metal phosphates that exhibit desirable properties such as high energy storage capacity combined with electrochem. stability.  Only one member of this class-the olivine LiFePO4 (ref. 3)-has risen to prominence so far, owing to its other characteristics, which include low cost, low environmental impact and safety.  These are crit. for large-capacity systems such as plug-in hybrid elec. vehicles.  Nonetheless, olivine has some inherent shortcomings, including 1-dimensional lithium-ion transport and a two-phase redox reaction that together limit the mobility of the phase boundary.  Thus, nanocrystallites are key to enable fast rate behavior.  Also probably the long-term economic viability of large-scale Li-ion energy storage systems could be ultimately limited by global lithium reserves, although this remains speculative at present.  Here, the authors report on a sodium/lithium iron phosphate, A2FePO4F (A = Na, Li), that could serve as a cathode in either Li-ion or Na-ion cells.  Also, it possesses facile two-dimensional pathways for Li+ transport, and the structural changes on redn.-oxidn. are minimal.  This results in a vol. change of only 3.7% that, unlike the olivine, contributes to the absence of distinct two-phase behavior during redox, and a reversible capacity that is 85% of theor.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoHTt31WeplQLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljwAeYjT41Rvg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2sXhtFWhtrjO&amp;md5=00fab3305d4ec64c11da74f07ad1ff76</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40914g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tripathi, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wood, S.                         cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion mobility in layered Na2FePO4F and olivine Na[Fe,Mn]PO4</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tripathi, R.; Wood, S. M.; Islam, M. S.; Nazar, L. F.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2257-2264</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The Na-ion battery is currently the focus of much research interest due to its cost advantages and the relative abundance of sodium as compared to lithium.  Olivine NaMPO4 (M = Fe, Fe0.5Mn0.5, Mn) and layered Na2FePO4F are interesting materials that have been reported recently as attractive pos. electrodes.  Here, we report their Na-ion conduction behavior and intrinsic defect properties using atomistic simulation methods.  In the olivines, Na ion migration is essentially restricted to the [010] direction along a curved trajectory, similar to that of LiMPO4, but with a lower migration energy (0.3 eV).  However, Na/M antisite defects are also predicted to have a lower formation energy: the higher probability of tunnel occupation with a relatively immobile M2+ cation - along with a greater vol. change on redox cycling - contributes to the poor electrochem. performance of the Na-olivine.  Na+ ion conduction in Na2FePO4F is predicted to be two-dimensional (2D) in the interlayer plane with a similar low activation energy.  The antisite formation energy is slightly higher; furthermore, antisite occupation would not be predicted to impede transport significantly owing to the 2D pathway.  This factor, along with the much lower vol. change on redox cycling, is undoubtedly responsible for the better electrochem. performance of the layered structure.  Where vol. change and structural effects do not incur impediments, Na-ion materials may present significant advantages over their Li counterparts.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpfJmYYqUdmsLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljwAeYjT41Rvg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtFOisrzP&amp;md5=e6e9205295df11e3a065bc2b66053479</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1021/ic100583f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Barpanda, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chotard, J.                         cas_cit31R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">31</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structural, Transport, and Electrochemical Investigation of Novel AMSO4F (A = Na, Li; M = Fe, Co, Ni, Mn) Metal Fluorosulphates Prepared Using Low Temperature Synthesis Routes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Barpanda, Prabeer; Chotard, Jean-Noel; Recham, Nadir; Delacourt, Charles; Ati, Mohamed; Dupont, Loic; Armand, Michel; Tarascon, Jean-Marie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Inorganic Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors have recently reported a promising 3.6 V metal fluorosulfate (LiFeSO4F) electrode, capable of high capacity, rate capability, and cycling stability.  In the current work, the authors extend the fluorosulfate chem. from Li to Na-based systems.  In this venture, the authors have reported the synthesis and crystal structure of NaMSO4F candidates for the 1st time.  As opposed to the triclinic-based LiMSO4F phases, the NaMSO4F phases adopt a monoclinic structure.  The authors further report the degree and possibility of forming Na(Fe1-xMx)SO4F and (Na1-xLix)MSO4F (M = Fe, Co, Ni) solid-soln. phases for the 1st time.  Relying on the underlying topochem. reaction, the authors have successfully synthesized the NaMSO4F, Na(Fe1-xMx)SO4F, and (Na1-xLix)MSO4F products at a low temp. of 300° using both ionothermal and solid-state syntheses.  The crystal structure, thermal stability, ionic cond., and reactivity of these new phases toward Li and Na were studied.  Among them, NaFeSO4F is the only one to present some redox activity (Fe2+/Fe3+) toward Li at 3.6 V.  Addnl., this phase shows a pressed-pellet ionic cond. of 10-7 S cm-1.  These findings further illustrate the richness of the fluorosulfate crystal chem., which has just been recently unveiled.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpledZP58tsrbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljwAeYjT41Rvg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXovFegtrg%253D&amp;md5=5b22e8f5c5729c380e1467f491b5a8cb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2016.04.138"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manikandan, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ramasubramonian, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shaijumon, M.                         cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Layered P2-type Na0.5Ni0.25Mn0.75O2 as a high performance cathode material for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Manikandan, P.; Ramasubramonian, D.; Shaijumon, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">There is a great interest in developing efficient electrode materials for Na-ion batteries, which are currently being projected as a promising alternative to Li-ion batteries.  Here the authors report the synthesis of a Na-ion battery cathode material, Na0.5Ni0.25Mn0.75O2, through a sol-gel method.  The as-synthesized layered Na0.5Ni0.25Mn0.75O2 material exhibits a layered P2-type structure, and shows excellent electrochem. properties when studied as a cathode for Na-ion batteries, with a specific discharge capacity of 210 mAh g-1 and a good capacity retention of 80% at 50th cycle in the voltage range from 1.5 to 4.4 V at 0.1 C. Recently, research and development attempts toward a room-temp. seawater battery (SWB) have been rejuvenated, as SWBs are considered capable, low-cost substitutes to the existing Li-ion battery technol. for large-scale usage.  In this work, P2-type layered Na0.5Co0.5Mn0.5O2 oxide was discovered for the first time to be a new option of a bifunctional cathode electrocatalyst material for an aq. SWB, with two kinds of voltage profiles, a slope and flat voltage region, corresponding to the Na+ ion intercalation-deintercalation reaction and OER/ORR catalytic activities in comparison with a carbon felt substrate and 20% Pt/C catalyst.                         cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Insights into the Dual-Electrode Characteristics of Layered Na0.5Ni0.25Mn0.75O2 Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palanisamy, Manikandan; Kim, Hyun Woo; Heo, Seongwoo; Lee, Eungje; Kim, Youngsik</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">10618-10625</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are now close to replacing lithium-ion batteries because they provide superior alternative energy storage solns. that are in great demand, particularly for large-scale applications.  To that end, the present study is focused on the properties of a new type of dual-electrode material, Na0.5Ni0.25Mn0.75O2, synthesized using a mixed hydroxy-carbonate route.  Cyclic voltammetry confirms that redox couples, at high and low voltage ranges, are facilitated by the unique features and properties of this dual-electrode, through sodium ion deintercalation/intercalation into the layered Na0.5Ni0.25Mn0.75O2 material.  This material provides superior performance for Na-ion batteries, as evidenced by the fabricated sodium cell that yielded initial charge-discharge capacities of 125/218 mAh/g in the voltage range of 1.5-4.4 V at 0.5 C.  At a low voltage range (1.5-2.6 V), the anode cell delivered discharge-charge capacities of 100/99 mAh/g with 99% capacity retention, which corresponds to highly reversible redox reaction of the Mn4+/3+ redn. and the Mn3+/4+ oxidn. obsd. at 1.85 and 2.06 V, resp.  The sym. Na-ion cell, fabricated using Na0.5Ni0.25Mn0.75O2, yielded initial charge-discharge capacities of 196/187 μAh at 107 μA.  These results encourage the further development of new types of futuristic sodium-ion-battery-based energy storage systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqZ2aXtQarB7LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgryHjVhBQYDA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXktVShs7c%253D&amp;md5=966da3183569d4575459edf39702de0a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit36" data-doi="10.1038/nmat3964"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chisnell, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ong, S.
