                10.1038/nmat3964</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D13%26publication_year%3D2014%26pages%3D586-592%26journal%3DNat.%2BMater.%26issue%3D6%26author%3DX.%2BLi%26author%3DX.%2BMa%26author%3DD.%2BSu%26author%3DL.%2BLiu%26author%3DR.%2BChisnell%26author%3DS.%2BP.%2BOng%26author%3DH.%2BChen%26author%3DA.%2BToumar%26author%3DJ.%2BC.%2BIdrobo%26author%3DY.%2BLei%26author%3DJ.%2BBai%26author%3DF.%2BWang%26author%3DJ.%2BW.%2BLynn%26author%3DY.%2BS.%2BLee%26author%3DG.%2BCeder%26title%3DDirect%2BVisualization%2Bof%2Bthe%2BJahn-Teller%2BEffect%2BCoupled%2Bto%2BNa%2BOrdering%2Bin%2BNa5%252F8MnO2%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fnmat3964&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c04482&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fnmat3964&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit36R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">36</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Direct visualization of the Jahn-Teller effect coupled to Na ordering in Na5/8MnO2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Xin; Ma, Xiaohua; Su, Dong; Liu, Lei; Chisnell, Robin; Ong, Shyue Ping; Chen, Hailong; Toumar, Alexandra; Idrobo, Juan-Carlos; Lei, Yuechuan; Bai, Jianming; Wang, Feng; Lynn, Jeffrey W.; Lee, Young S.; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">586-592</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NMAACR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1476-1122</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The cooperative Jahn-Teller effect (CJTE) refers to the correlation of distortions arising from individual Jahn-Teller centers in complex compds.  The effect usually induces strong coupling between the static or dynamic charge, orbital and magnetic ordering, which was related to many important phenomena such as colossal magnetoresistance and supercond.  The authors report a Na5/8MnO2 superstructure with a pronounced static CJTE that is coupled to an unusual Na vacancy ordering.  The authors visualize this coupled distortion and Na ordering down to the at. scale.  The Mn planes are periodically distorted by a charge modulation on the Mn stripes, which in turn drives an unusually large displacement of some Na ions through long-ranged Na-O-Mn3+-O-Na interactions into a highly distorted octahedral site.  At lower temps., magnetic order appears, in which Mn at. stripes with different magnetic couplings are interwoven with each other.  The authors' work demonstrates the strong interaction between alkali ordering, displacement, and electronic and magnetic structure, and underlines the important role that structural details play in detg. electronic behavior.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp0lApkLYbdH7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgryHjVhBQYDA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXotFeqsbc%253D&amp;md5=8498b87216c43857b7fa211e188a78a5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee41031e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palomares, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Casas-Cabanas, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Castillo-Martínez, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  This work presents an up-to-date information on Na-based battery materials.  On the one hand, it explores the feasibility of two novel energy storage systems: Na-aq. batteries and Na-O2 technol.  On the other hand, it summarizes new advances on non-aq. Na-ion systems.  Although all of them can be placed under the umbrella of Na-based systems, aq. and oxygen-based batteries are arising technologies with increasing significance in energy storage research, while non-aq. sodium-ion technol. has become one of the most important research lines in this field.  These systems meet different requirements of energy storage: Na-aq. batteries will have a detg. role as a low cost and safer technol.; Na-O2 systems can be the key technol. to overcome the need for high energy d. storage devices; and non-aq. Na-ion batteries have application in the field of stationary energy storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqoMIYpy9lmPbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgryHjVhBQYDA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtFOisr%252FF&amp;md5=2cb5179714ac258186fecdd5b6c69452</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit38" data-doi="10.1039/C4EE00465E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Billaud, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Singh, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Armstrong, A.                         cas_cit38R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">38</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na0.67Mn1-xMgxO2 (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.2): a high capacity cathode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Billaud, Juliette; Singh, Gurpreet; Armstrong, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Earth-abundant Na0.67[Mn1-xMgx]O2 (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.2) cathode materials with the P2 structure have been synthesized as pos. electrodes for sodium-ion batteries.  Na0.67MnO2 exhibits a capacity of 175 mA h g-1 with good capacity retention.  A Mg content of 5% is sufficient to smooth the charge/discharge profiles without affecting the capacity, while further increasing the Mg content improves the cycling stability, but at the expense of a lower discharge capacity (∼150 mA h g-1 for Na0.67Mn0.8Mg0.2O2).  It was obsd. that the cooling process during synthesis, as well as Mg content, have an influence on the structure.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoIHzGiuvAj8LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgryHjVhBQYDA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXltFWmsrw%253D&amp;md5=aa8e41ccefb89becb873e12ca8bb7d32</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref39"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit39" data-doi="10.1039/c2cp43377j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ong, S.                         cas_cit39R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">39</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A comparison of destabilization mechanisms of the layered NaxMO2 and LixMO2 compounds upon alkali de-intercalation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sangtae; Ma, Xiaohua; Ong, Shyue Ping; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">44</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">15571-15578</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PPCPFQ</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To understand the difference in reversible energy storage capacity between the O3-type layered Na and Li compds., the first principles calcns. are used to study and contrast the effect of two well-known destabilization mechanisms, transformation into the spinel-type structures and cation mixing due to transition metal migration.  This study is performed on the layered oxides at the A0.5MO2 compn., where A = (Na, Li) and M is a 3d transition metal.  It is found that while all Li0.5MO2 compds. have strong driving forces and low energy kinetic paths to transform to the spinel structure, Na0.5MO2 compds. do not have thermodn. driving forces to transform to spinel type structures.  It is also found that transition metal mobility is higher in Li layered compds. than in Na layered compds. because of the unusual activated state for transition metal hopping.  For many compds., migration goes along an oct-tet-oct path, but transition metal migration needs to be assisted by alkali migration into a tetrahedral site forming activated Atet-Mtet defects; substituting Na for Li in the layered structure results in increased transition metal migration barriers due to the larger size of Na+ ions.  Overall, the findings indicate that Na compds. in the layered O3 structure have fundamentally different destabilization mechanisms to those of Li compds.  This distinction allows superior battery electrode performance in many Na compds. and offers optimistic perspective on finding many high energy d. Na electrodes that cycle with stable high capacity.                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis and Stoichiometry of Different Layered Sodium Cobalt Oxides</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lei, Yuechuan; Li, Xin; Liu, Lei; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">26</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">18</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5288-5296</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered Na-metal oxides can form in different crystal structures, each with different electrochem. behavior.  As a prototype system to better understand how each phase can be formed, we present the conditions under which different layered phases of NaxCoO2 can be stabilized in solid-state synthesis.  Using a novel combination of ex situ XRD on as-synthesized samples, with in situ XRD to monitor the relation between Na content and lattice parameters, we are able to construct a phase diagram of NaxCoO2 between 450 to 750 °C in air and for Na:Co sample ratios ranging from 0.60 to 1.05.  Four single phase domains of O3, O'3, P'3, and P2 are revealed based on the XRD anal.  In contrast to previous reports it is found that pure O3, O'3 and P'3 phase can only form at a fixed stoichiometry of x = 1.00, 0.83, and 0.67, resp., while the P2 phase forms in a slightly larger compn. range from 0.68 to 0.76.  Galvanostatic charging of O3-Na1.00CoO2 shows several flat and sloping regions on the voltage profile, which follows the sequence of O3-O'3-P'3-P3-P'3, with increasing interslab distances.  Our results indicate that the electrochem. important P2 structure is likely stabilized by entropy.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrv-QfiXqid1rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgnLE6FSrmG0w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhsVyjtL%252FI&amp;md5=da9f99c6485a2fb393272697bd36e7f8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit41" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201400083"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hasa, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Scrosati, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hassoun, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High Performance Na<sub>0.5</sub>[Ni<sub>0.23</sub>Fe<sub>0.13</sub>Mn<sub>0.63</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical and in-situ x-ray diffraction studies of lithium intercalation in lithium cobalt oxide (LixCoO2)</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Reimers, Jan N.; Dahn, J. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1992</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">139</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2091-7</span>CODEN:

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. properties of LixCoO2 are studied as Li is deintercalated from LiCoO2.  High-precision voltage measurements and in-situ x-ray diffraction indicate a sequence of 3 distinct phase transitions as x varies from 1 to 0.4.  Two of the transitions are situated slightly above and below x = 1/2 and are caused by an order/disorder transition of the Li ions.  The order/disorder transition was studied as a function of temp., allowing the detn. of an order/disorder phase diagram.  In-situ x-ray diffraction measurements facilitate a direct observation of the effects of deintercalation on the host lattice crystal structure.  The other phase transition is 1st order (coexisting phases are obsd. for 0.75 ≤ x ≤ 0.93) involving a significant expansion of the c-lattice parameter of the hexagonal unit cell.  The authors report the variation of the lattice consts. of LixCoO2 with x and show that the phase transition to the Li ordered phase near x = 1/2 is accompanied by a lattice distortion to a monoclinic unit cell with aMon 4.865(2), bMon 2.806(1), cMon 14.420(4) Å and β 90.77 (3).  The authors report an overall phase diagram for 0.4 ≤ x ≤ 1.0 and -10° ≤ T ≤ 60°.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpVKMS8PYG-U7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgnLE6FSrmG0w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK38Xms1Sgsbw%253D&amp;md5=374b51741ab8cf4edb14f58fa3b82675</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1039/c1ee01782a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ong, S.                         cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Voltage, stability and diffusion barrier differences between sodium-ion and lithium-ion intercalation materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ong, Shyue Ping; Chevrier, Vincent L.; Hautier, Geoffroy; Jain, Anubhav; Moore, Charles; Kim, Sangtae; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3680-3688</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To evaluate the potential of Na-ion batteries, we contrast in this work the difference between Na-ion and Li-ion based intercalation chemistries in terms of three key battery properties-voltage, phase stability and diffusion barriers.  The compds. investigated comprise the layered AMO2 and AMS2 structures, the olivine and maricite AMPO4 structures, and the NASICON A3V2(PO4)3 structures.  The calcd. Na voltages for the compds. investigated are 0.18-0.57 V lower than that of the corresponding Li voltages, in agreement with previous exptl. data.  We believe the obsd. lower voltages for Na compds. are predominantly a cathodic effect related to the much smaller energy gain from inserting Na into the host structure compared to inserting Li.  We also found a relatively strong dependence of battery properties on structural features.  In general, the difference between the Na and Li voltage of the same structure, ΔVNa-Li, is less neg. for the maricite structures preferred by Na, and more neg. for the olivine structures preferred by Li.  The layered compds. have the most neg. ΔVNa-Li.  In terms of phase stability, we found that open structures, such as the layered and NASICON structures, that are better able to accommodate the larger Na+ ion generally have both Na and Li versions of the same compd.  For the close-packed AMPO4 structures, our results show that Na generally prefers the maricite structure, while Li prefers the olivine structure, in agreement with previous exptl. work.  We also found surprising evidence that the barriers for Na+ migration can potentially be lower than that for Li+ migration in the layered structures.  Overall, our findings indicate that Na-ion systems can be competitive with Li-ion systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGosFyTQ7K1B3LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgnLE6FSrmG0w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXht1CqsLzK&amp;md5=5a6e72a3ae5ebf92d84322240b2c29cc</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit44" data-doi="10.1149/1.2095407"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shacklette, L.     </div><div class="casAbstract">NaxCoO2 bronze was prepd. in 4 different phases: O3, O'3, P3, and P2 (O = octahedral and P = trigonal prismatic and 3 or 2 represents the no. of distinguishable Na layers), and the phase evolution and capacity of these bronzes were investigated during Na+ insertion and extn. in nonaq. electrolyte. The measured capacity and discharge voltage are equiv. to 300 W-h/kg; a cycle life to 50% capacity is projected to be 340 cycles at 1 mA/cm2.  The bronze prepd. in the P2 phase exhibits thermodn. reversible behavior at 2.2-4.0 V.                         cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical investigation of the P2-NaxCoO2 phase diagram</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Berthelot, R.; Carlier, D.; Delmas, C.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">74-80</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na layered oxides NaxCoO2 form one of the most fascinating low-dimensional and strongly correlated systems; in particular P2-NaxCoO2 exhibits various single-phase domains with different Na+/vacancy patterns depending on the Na concn.  Here the authors used Na batteries to clearly depict the P2-NaxCoO2 phase diagram for x ≥ 0.50.  By coupling the electrochem. process with an in situ x-ray diffraction expt., the authors identified the succession of single-phase or two-phase domains appearing on Na intercalation with a rather good accuracy compared with previous studies.  The authors reported new single-phase domains and the authors underlined the thermal instability of some ordered phases from an electrochem. study at various temps.  As each phase was characterized by the position of its Fermi level vs. the Na+/Na couple, the synthesis of each material, even in large amts., can be carried out electrochem.  The phys. properties of the as-prepd. Na1/2CoO2 and Na2/3CoO2 ordered phases were characterized and compared.  Electrochem. processes are an accurate route to precisely study in a continuous way such a complex system and provide a new way to synthesize materials with a very narrow existence range.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrk1JcT1P9myrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgadJVp7dA00A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXhsFKisrvJ&amp;md5=d9d06332c38e3009393054b672c767f7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47" data-doi="10.1021/cm3029615"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Moretti, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kloepsch, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nowak, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Siozios, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Winter, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Toward Na-Ion Batteries - Synthesis and Characterization of a Novel High Capacity Na Ion Intercalation Material</span>. <i>Chem.                         cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Toward Na-ion Batteries - Synthesis and Characterization of a Novel High Capacity Na Ion Intercalation Material</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Buchholz, Daniel; Moretti, Arianna; Kloepsch, Richard; Nowak, Sascha; Siozios, Vassilios; Winter, Martin; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">25</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">142-148</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The rapid growth of the worldwide demand of lithium for batteries (lithium-ion batteries) can possibly lead to a shortage of its reserves.  Sodium batteries represent a promising alternative because they enable much higher energy densities than other battery systems, with the exception of lithium-ion batteries, and are not limited by sodium availability.  Herein, we present a novel, Na+ ion intercalation material, Na0.45Ni0.22Co0.11Mn0.66O2 (space group P63/mmc) synthesized in air by a copptn. method followed by a thermal treatment and a water-rinsing step.  This material has a sp. capacity of 135 mA-h/g with a Coulombic efficiency &gt;99.7%.  Upon long-term cycling tests the material shows excellent capacity retention after more than 250 cycles.  Such an overall performance, superior to that of presently known sodium-ion cathodes, represents a step further toward the realization of sustainable batteries for efficient stationary energy storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp4KwnjmRqKGrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgadJVp7dA00A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhvVCqs7rM&amp;md5=bdce54d96eddec91a8df71b97e0f3f1d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1021/am5080437"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hasa, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hassoun, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Comparative Study of Layered Transition Metal Oxide Cathodes for Application in Sodium-Ion Battery</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Comparative study of layered transition metal oxide cathodes for application in sodium-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hasa, Ivana; Buchholz, Daniel; Passerini, Stefano; Hassoun, Jusef</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Herein, we report a study on P-type layered sodium transition metal-based oxides with a general formula of NaxMO2 (M = Ni, Fe, Mn).  We synthesize the materials via copptn. followed by annealing in air and rinsing with water, and we examine the electrodes as cathodes for sodium-ion batteries using a propylene carbonate-based electrolyte.  We fully investigate the effect of the Ni-to-Fe ratio, annealing temp., and sodium content on the electrochem. performances of the electrodes.  The impact of these parameters on the structural and electrochem. properties of the materials is revealed by X-ray diffraction, SEM, and cyclic voltammetry, resp.                         cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structural characterization of layered Na0.5Co0.5Mn0.5O2 material as a promising cathode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Manikandan, Palanisamy; Heo, Seongwoo; Kim, Hyun Woo; Jeong, Hu Young; Lee, Eungje; Kim, Youngsik</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered Na0.5Co0.5Mn0.5O2 material is synthesized through a facile mixed hydroxy-carbonate route using (Co0.5Mn0.5)2(OH)2CO3 precursor and well characterized as a hexagonal layered structure under P63/mmc space group.                         cas_cit50R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">50</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Oxygen-Release-Related Thermal Stability and Decomposition Pathways of LixNi0.5Mn1.5O4 Cathode Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Enyuan; Bak, Seong-Min; Liu, Jue; Yu, Xiqian; Zhou, Yongning; Ehrlich, Steven N.; Yang, Xiao-Qing; Nam, Kyung-Wan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The thermal stability of charged cathode materials is one of the crit. properties affecting the safety characteristics of lithium-ion batteries.  New findings on the thermal-stability and thermal-decompn. pathways related to the oxygen release are discovered for the high-voltage spinel LixNi0.5Mn1.5O4 (LNMO) with ordered (o-) and disordered (d-) structures at the fully delithiated (charged) state using a combination of in situ time-resolved x-ray diffraction (TR-XRD) coupled with mass spectroscopy (MS) and X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) during heating.  Both o- and d- LixNi0.5Mn1.5O4, at their fully charged states, start oxygen-releasing structural changes at temps. &lt;300°, which is in sharp contrast to the good thermal stability of the 4V-spinel LixMn2O4 with no oxygen being released up to 375°.  This is mainly caused by the presence of Ni4+ in LNMO, which undergoes dramatic redn. during the thermal decompn.  Charged o-LNMO shows better thermal stability than the d-LNMO counterpart, due to the Ni/Mn ordering and smaller amt. of the rock-salt impurity phase in o-LNMO.  Two newly identified thermal-decompn. pathways from the initial LixNi0.5Mn1.5O4 spinel to the final NiMn2O4-type spinel structure with and without the intermediate phases (NiMnO3 and α-Mn2O3) play key roles in thermal stability and oxygen release of LNMO during thermal decompn.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpGL1gLyS4nx7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgadJVp7dA00A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhvFSqt73O&amp;md5=ec8ba0bce0e933c077888f2b35b761cf</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit51" data-doi="10.1021/cm303096e"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bak, S.                         cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Correlating Structural Changes and Gas Evolution during the Thermal Decomposition of Charged LixNi0.8Co0.15Al0.05O2 Cathode Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bak, Seong-Min; Nam, Kyung-Wan; Chang, Wonyoung; Yu, Xiqian; Hu, Enyuan; Hwang, Sooyeon; Stach, Eric A.; Kim, Kwang-Bum; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Yang, Xiao-Qing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, results are presented from the application of a new in situ technique that combines time-resolved synchrotron X-ray diffraction and mass spectroscopy.  This approach is exploited to provide direct correlation between structural changes and the evolution of gas that occurs during the thermal decompn. of (over)charged cathode materials used in lithium-ion batteries.  Results from charged LixNi0.8Co0.15Al0.05O2 cathode materials indicate that the evolution of both O2 and CO2 gases are strongly related to phase transitions that occur during thermal decompn., specifically from the layered structure (space group Fd‾3m) to the disordered spinel structure (Fd‾3m), and finally to the rock-salt structure (Fm‾3m).  The state of charge also significantly affects both the structural changes and the evolution of oxygen as the temp. increases: the more extensive the charge, the lower the temp. of the phase transitions and the larger the oxygen release.  Ex situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) and in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) are also utilized to investigate the local structural and valence state changes in Ni and Co ions, and to characterize microscopic morphol. changes.  The combination of these advanced tools provides a unique approach to study fundamental aspects of the dynamic phys. and chem. changes that occur during thermal decompn. of charged cathode materials in a systematic way.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp4aEXgK98Me7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj78eEdwx92Tg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXitVCjsg%253D%253D&amp;md5=1c51181dee45cbde4a4f7e6b77191ff0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit52" data-doi="10.1149/2.002201esl"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahn, J.                         cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NaCrO2 cathode for high-rate sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yu, Chan-Yeop; Park, Jae-Sang; Jung, Hun-Gi; Chung, Kyung-Yoon; Aurbach, Doron; Sun, Yang-Kook; Myung, Seung-Taek</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2019-2026</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries offer a potential alternative or complementary system to lithium-ion batteries, which are widely used in many applications.  For this purpose, layered O3-type NaCrO2 for use as a cathode material in sodium-ion batteries was synthesized via an emulsion-drying method.  The produced NaCrO2 was modified using pitch as a carbon source and the products were tested in half and full cells using a NaPF6-based non-aq. electrolyte soln.  The carbon-coated NaCrO2 cathode material exhibits excellent capacity retention with superior rate capability up to a rate of 150 C (99 mA h (g oxide)-1), which corresponds to full discharge during 27 s.  The surface conducting carbon layer plays a critically important role in the excellent performance of this cathode material.  We confirmed the reaction process with sodium using X-ray diffraction and X-ray absorption spectroscopy.  Thermal anal. using time-resolved X-ray diffraction also demonstrated the structural stability of carbon-coated Na0.5CrO2.  Due to the excellent performance of the cathode material described herein, this study has the potential to promote the accelerated development of sodium-ion batteries for a large no. of applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdoV2EZhlfBrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj78eEdwx92Tg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXosVKgtLg%253D&amp;md5=4a8043bc0b2e1d073e5df56ab1b59e77</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit54" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b09280"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ikeuchi, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Thermal Stability of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CrO<sub>2</sub> for Rechargeable Sodium Batteries; Studies by High-Temperature Synchrotron X-Ray Diffraction</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit54R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">54</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Thermal Stability of NaxCrO2 for Rechargeable Sodium Batteries; Studies by High-Temperature Synchrotron X-ray Diffraction</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Ikeuchi, Issei; Kubota, Kei; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Thermal stability and phase transition processes of NaCrO2 and Na0.5CrO2 are carefully examd. by high-temp. synchrotron X-ray diffraction method.  O3-type NaCrO2 shows anisotropic thermal expansion on heating, which is a common character as layered materials, without phase transition in the temp. range of 27-527 °C.  In contrast, for the desodiated phase, in-plane distorted P3-type layered oxide (P'3 Na0.5CrO2), phase transition occurs in the following order.  Monoclinic distortion assocd. with Na/vacancy ordering is gradually lost on heating, and its symmetry increases and changes to a rhombohedral lattice at 207 °C.  On further heating, phase segregation to two P3 layered metastable phases, which have different interlayer distances (17.0 and 13.5 Å, presumably sodium-rich and sodium-free P3 phases, resp.) are obsd. on heating to 287-477 °C, but oxygen loss is not obsd.  Oxygen loss is obsd. at temps. only above 500 °C, resulting in the formation of corundum-type Cr2O3 and O3 NaCrO2 as thermodynamically stable phases.  From these results, possibility of NaxCrO2 as a pos. electrode material for safe rechargeable sodium batteries is also discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqIaUJLOGoDTbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj78eEdwx92Tg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhvVSjur%252FP&amp;md5=a0700e6ef11d18eb49cdfffae068f68c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit55" data-doi="10.1149/2.007305jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dimov, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Okada, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nishida, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical and Thermal Properties of α-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> Cathode for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical and thermal properties of α-NaFeO2 cathode for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Jie; Zhao, Liwei; Dimov, Nikolay; Okada, Shigeto; Nishida, Tetsuaki</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">160</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">α-NaFeO2 is promising as minor-metal free cathode materials for low cost sodium-ion batteries.  It has a flat voltage plateau at 3.3 V vs. Na metal and a stable reversible capacity of 85 mAh g-1.  Fe3+/Fe4+ redox reaction on charge/discharge cycle was confirmed by 57Fe Mossbauer spectrometer.  The thermal stability of NaFeO2 cathodes with/without 1 mol dm-3 NaClO4/EC-DMC electrolyte was investigated by DSC measurements.  The fully-charged Na0.58FeO2 powder decompd. thermally at a temp. higher than 300°C, with Fe2O3 as a possible product.  On the other hand, the mixt. of Na0.58FeO2 powder and electrolyte showed exothermic heat in a temp. range of 220-300°C.  However, NaFeO2 showed better thermal stability in the electrolyte than LiCoO2 counterparts in Li ion battery systems, including less heat generation and higher exothermic onset temp.  This indicated that the Na-ion batteries might have comparable thermal stability with Li-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp7pbom46LvHbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj78eEdwx92Tg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXptlKqs74%253D&amp;md5=62bf0f1e2d0f58000d6284d91288c0bd</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit56" data-doi="10.1149/2.102205jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahn, J.                         cas_cit56R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">56</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Study of the Reactivity of De-Intercalated P2-NaxCoO2 with Non-Aqueous Solvent and Electrolyte by Accelerating Rate Calorimetry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xia, Xin; Dahn, J. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">159</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A647-A650</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">P2-type layered structure NaxCoO2 (x∼0.65) was successfully synthesized by solid state reaction.  The electrochem. performance of Na0.65CoO2 as a pos. electrode material for sodium-ion batteries was tested at room temp. using two-electrode coin cells with NaClO4/PC electrolyte.  Na0.65CoO2 delivered a reversible capacity of 70 mAh/g during the first charge to 4.3 V, which is related to the de-intercalation of about 0.3 mol Na per mol. Co.  The reactivity of de-intercalated NaxCoO2 (∼ Na0.35CoO2) with ethylene carbonate/diethyl carbonate (EC:DEC) solvent and with NaPF6/EC:DEC electrolyte was studied using accelerating rate calorimetry (ARC).  X-ray diffraction was used to study the products of the reactions of Na0.35CoO2 with solvent or electrolyte after the ARC expts.  De-intercalated NaxCoO2 shows high reactivity in both solvent and electrolyte.  Na0.35CoO2 reacts with solvent at elevated temp. in a manner which is similar to de-intercalated LixCoO2, leading to the products Na0.7CoO2 and Co3O4.  Then Na0.7CoO2 reacts to form Co3O4 and NaHCO3.  Then Co3O4 reacts to form CoO or Co metal.  In NaPF6-based electrolyte, the reaction mechanism changes completely as the major reaction products are NaCoF3 and CoO or Co.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrYh-KbBaFaQrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj78eEdwx92Tg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XksVKqurw%253D&amp;md5=48a84be619e43fcbcfcaaedb0cf45578</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref57"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">57</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit57" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b04478"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jo, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chung, K.                         cas_cit57R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">57</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Investigation of Thermal Stability of P2-NaxCoO2 Cathode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries Using Real-Time Electron Microscopy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Sooyeon; Lee, Yongho; Jo, Eunmi; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Choi, Wonchang; Kim, Seung Min; Chang, Wonyoung</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Here, we take advantage of in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) to investigate the thermal stability of P2-type NaxCoO2 cathode materials for sodium ion batteries, which are promising candidates for next-generation lithium ion batteries.  A double-tilt TEM heating holder was used to directly characterize the changes in the morphol. and the crystallog. and electronic structures of the materials with increase in temp.  The electron diffraction patterns and the electron energy loss spectra demonstrated the presence of cobalt oxides (Co3O4, CoO) and even metallic cobalt (Co) at higher temps. as a result of redn. of Co ions and loss of oxygen.  The bright-field TEM images revealed that the surface of NaxCoO2 becomes porous at high temps.  Higher cutoff voltages result in degrading thermal stability of NaxCoO2.  The observations herein provide a valuable insight that thermal stability is one of the important factors to be considered in addn. to the electrochem. properties when developing new electrode materials for novel battery systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpmdqVISMboDrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj1jHT4iaLrLA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXnsVyqu7w%253D&amp;md5=dc97b317173f63a00e6baee5ff0b506a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit58" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.                         cas_cit58R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">58</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Sung-Wook; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Ma, Xiaohua; Ceder, Gerbrand; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li)-ion batteries (LIB) have governed the current worldwide rechargeable battery market due to their outstanding energy and power capability.  In particular, the LIB's role in enabling elec. vehicles (EVs) has been highlighted to replace the current oil-driven vehicles in order to reduce the usage of oil resources and generation of CO2 gases.  Unlike Li, sodium is one of the more abundant elements on Earth and exhibits similar chem. properties to Li, indicating that Na chem. could be applied to a similar battery system.  In the 1970s-80s, both Na-ion and Li-ion electrodes were investigated, but the higher energy d. of Li-ion cells made them more applicable to small, portable electronic devices, and research efforts for rechargeable batteries have been mainly concd. on LIB since then.  Recently, research interest in Na-ion batteries (NIB) has been resurrected, driven by new applications with requirements different from those in portable electronics, and to address the concern on Li abundance.  In this article, both neg. and pos. electrode materials in NIB are briefly reviewed.  While the voltage is generally lower and the vol. change upon Na removal or insertion is larger for Na-intercalation electrodes, compared to their Li equiv., the power capability can vary depending on the crystal structures.  It is concluded that cost-effective NIB can partially replace LIB, but requires further investigation and improvement.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGonnrfiSUpaRrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj1jHT4iaLrLA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhtVOrtLjF&amp;md5=485b20393bb8876b2f69ca708aeba606</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref59"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">59</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit59" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA03173G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rami

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na Co oxide (NCO) was synthesized by a glycine assisted sol-gel combustion method.  XRD studies confirm the P2 phase formation of NCO.         </div><div class="casAbstract">NaxCoO2 is a commonly used cathode material for sodium ion batteries because of its easy synthesis, high reversible capacity and good cyclability.  The structural and electrochem. properties of NaxCoO2 during sodium ion insertion/extn. process are studied based on first principles calcns.  The calcn. results of crystal structure parameters and av. intercalation voltage are in good agreement with expt. data.  Through calcn. of the geometric structure and charge transfer in charging and discharging processes of NaxCoO2, it is found that the oxygen atom surrounding Co of the CoO6 octahedral screens the coulomb potential produced by sodium vacancy in NaxCoO2, and the charge is removed from the entire Co-O layer instead of the Co atom adjacent to sodium vacancy when sodium ions are extd. from the NaCoO2 lattice.                         cas_cit67R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">67</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Materials Design for High-Safety Sodium-Ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Chao; Xin, Sen; Mai, Liqiang; You, Ya</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries, with their evident superiority in resource abundance and cost, are emerging as promising next-generation energy storage systems for large-scale applications, such as smart grids and low-speed elec. vehicles.  Accidents related to fires and explosions for batteries are a reminder that safety is prerequisite for energy storage systems, esp. when aiming for grid-scale use.  In a typical electrochem. secondary battery, the elec. power is stored and released via processes that generate thermal energy, leading to temp. increments in the battery system, which is the main cause for battery thermal abuse.  The investigation of the energy generated during the chem./electrochem. reactions is of paramount importance for battery safety, unfortunately, it has not received the attention it deserves.  In this review, the fundamentals of the heat generation, accumulation, and transportation in a battery system are summarized and recent key research on materials design to improve sodium-ion battery safety is highlighted.  Several effective materials design concepts are also discussed.  This review is designed to arouse the attention of researcher and scholars and inspire further improvements in battery safety.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Structural confirmation of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material: (a) Thermogravimetric curve for the gel precursor of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material, recorded from room temperature to 900 °C at a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> under an air atmosphere. (b) Powder X-ray diffraction pattern of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material (calcined at 750 °C for 28 h) with Rietveld refinement analysis. Stacking arrangement of P2-type structure built by (c) hexagonal oxygen arrangement (red) with a cobalt atom (cyan) located at the center. (d) Three layer CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedra sheets were represented in AB|BA|AB close-packed arrangement with the sodium atom (gray) between two CoO<sub>6</sub> octahedra layers. (e) Schematic illustration of P2-type structure with MO<sub>6</sub> (octahedra) and NaO<sub>6</sub> (trigonal prisms) for the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c04482&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/medium/am1c04482_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0002.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr2" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Morphological investigation of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material: FESEM images of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material shown as (a) flaky sheets morphology and located by different alignments, (b) flaky sheets revealed with a hexagonal layered shape, and (c) flaky sheets stacked together and exhibited a flaky bundle morphology with a size of ∼300 nm (thickness); (d) FESEM electron image for the simultaneous mapping analyses of the respective quantified area; (e) simultaneous elemental mapping image, which confirmed the presence of (f) sodium (Na, cyan), (g) cobalt (Co, pink), and (h) oxygen (O, green) elements in the synthesized layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c04482&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/medium/am1c04482_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0003.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr3" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical characterization of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material: (a) sodium ion intercalation–deintercalation characteristics of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material examined by cyclic voltammetry technique between 2 and 4 V at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; voltage versus capacity profiles of sodium cells at (b) 0.5 C and (c) 1 C; (d) charge–discharge cycling studies of sodium cells for 1–100 cycles at 0.5 and 1 C; rate capability studies of (e) voltage versus capacity profiles and (f) capacity versus cycle number performance at different rates (0.1–5 C) in the voltage range 2–4 V using 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte in 1:1 EC–PC solvents.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c04482&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/medium/am1c04482_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0004.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr4" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. <i>In situ</i> thermal safety characteristics of layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material: (a) voltage versus capacity profiles of charge–discharge cycling studies between 2 and 4 V at 0.1 C; <i>in situ</i> MMC results for the sodium cell (b) charge state at 4 V, (c) discharge state at 2.8 V, and (d) discharge state at 2 V performed from 30 to 300 °C at a heating rate of 0.5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>, and the heat flux and the cell voltage relating to temperature and time were measured. The sodium cells were composed of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material, sodium metal, 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte in EC–PC solvents, glass fiber separator, and multiple CR-2032 coin cell components.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c04482&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig5"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/medium/am1c04482_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0005.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr5" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. <i>Ex situ</i> thermal safety characteristics of the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> cathode material: DSC spectra of (a) charge state at 2.8, 3.5, and 4 V and (b) discharge state at 3, 2.5, and 2 V for the layered P2-type NaCoO<sub>2</sub> material, represented with the heat flow versus temperature at an elevated temperature (50–325 °C). The insets #1 and #2 reveal the voltage versus capacity profiles of charge–discharge cycling studies between 2 and 4 V at 0.1 C to determine different states of charge and discharge conditions.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c04482/asset/images/large/am1c04482_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c04482&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></li><li id="pane-m2" aria-labelledby="pane-m2" class="tab__pane tab__pane-references"><div class="refs-header-label"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i16">References</h2></div></div></div><hr></div><p class="rightTab-references-count">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Researchers must find a sustainable way of providing the power our modern lifestyles demand.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrxUs7wptoM7LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhRmugFyz9_3A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1cXhs1Kntrc%253D&amp;md5=75c8cd94c450a1682aa6d1bd9bfe1c1f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202003668"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palanisamy, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Parekh, M. G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">In Situ Replenishment of Formation Cycle Lithium-Ion Loss for Enhancing Battery Life</span>. <i>Adv. Funct. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>30</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">46</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">2003668</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adfm.202003668</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In Situ Replenishment of Formation Cycle Lithium-Ion Loss for Enhancing Battery Life</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palanisamy, Manikandan; Parekh, Mihit H.; Pol, Vilas G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">30</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">46</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2003668</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In situ replenishment of formation cycle lithium-ion loss is considered for the development of longer-lasting rechargeable batteries, contg. a thin lithium reservoir-electrode to mitigate the formation cycle capacity loss.  Synchronized lithium and lithium-ion batteries (SLLIB) deliver specific charge-discharge capacities of 147/145 mAh g-1 for mesocarbon microbeads (MCMB) vs. LiFePO4 and 186/171 mAh g-1 for C-Si vs. LiNi1/3Mn1/3Co1/3O2 at 0.2 C.  The energy-reduced cells (due to solid electrolyte interface (SEI) formation) are replenished and achieved an increased energy d. of 455 Wh kg-1 with retained flat-voltage profile for SLLIB-MCMB vs. LiFePO4 and 576 Wh kg-1 for SLLIB-C-Si vs. LiNi1/3Mn1/3Co1/3O2 through Li+ ion in situ reservoir replenishment from the reservoir-electrode to the MCMB/C-Si anode.  Furthermore, reserve mode cycling process delivers charge-discharge capacities of 126/124 mAh g-1 for SLLIB-Li vs. LiFePO4 and 137/136 mAh g-1 for SLLIB-Li vs. LiNi1/3Mn1/3Co1/3O2 at 0.2 C, assocd. with the Li+ ion transportation between reservoir-electrode and LiFePO4/LiNi1/3Mn1/3Co1/3O2 cathode, confirmed by the Li+ ion diffusion path through MCMB and C-Si electrodes, using EIS anal.  Ultimately, the in situ reservoir replenishment, replenished cycling and reserve mode cycling processes of SLLIB leads to enhanced battery life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpxZuRQWLsAULVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhRmugFyz9_3A"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhvVajsbfE&amp;md5=a08c2e0d91684e5896a4856774cecb85</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1039/c3ta13621c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manikandan, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Periasamy, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jagannathan, R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sol-Gel Synthesis and Impedance Characteristics of Networked Nanocrystalline Olivine Cathode for Li-Ion Full Cells</span>. <i>J. Mater. Chem. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">48</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">15397</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">15405</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c3ta13621c</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sol-gel synthesis and impedance characteristics of networked nanocrystalline olivine cathode for Li-ion full cells</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Manikandan, P.; Periasamy, P.; Jagannathan, R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">48</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">15397-15405</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A sol-gel synthesis using adipic acid yielded small particles of ∼20 nm in size of olivine LiFePO4/C cathode materials.  In the characterization of cathode system(s), solid state impedance spectra of the pristine LiFePO4/C cathode revealed clear localization of charge through charge build-up.  When networked with MWCNT, this material facilitates enhancement in charge mobility, eventually explaining the capacity enhancement of the LiFePO4/C-MWCNT electrode, which yields a high capacity of 163 mA-h/g at C/10.  On the other hand, the lower capacity of 125 mA-h/g found for the pristine LiFePO4/C electrode material can be explained in terms of charge becoming localized/trapped in the vicinity of inter- and intra-granular regions of the cathode particles.  To get a broader view of the application potential (in terms of cell voltages of ∼3 V, 2 V, and safety aspects) of the networked cathode materials, two kinds of Li-ion full cells using mesocarbon microbeads and Li4Ti5O12 as anodes were fabricated, which yielded capacities of 1.94 and 2.1 mA-h, resp.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo0g3Q9zpxk67Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lig0RoNG5E2qA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhvVWmsb3O&amp;md5=5a623debb4d3f324828b7af879d4c497</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C4RA09400J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Manikandan, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Periasamy, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jagannathan, R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Nano-Granular Sn Impregnated NiTi Alloy Matrix Anode for High Voltage Li-Ion Pouch Cells</span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">5</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">3844</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3853</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Herein, we explore the electrochem. mechanism of a novel rechargeable seawater battery system that uses seawater as the cathode material.  Sodium is harvested from seawater while charging the battery, and the harvested sodium is discharged with oxygen dissolved in the seawater, functioning as oxidants to produce electricity.  The seawater provides both anode (Na metal) and cathode (O2) materials for the proposed battery.  Based on the discharge voltage (∼2.9 V) with participation of O2 and the charge voltage (∼4.1 V) with Cl2 evolution during the first cycle, a voltage efficiency of about 73 % is obtained.  If the seawater battery is constructed using hard carbon as the anode and a Na super ion conductor as the solid electrolyte, a strong cycle performance of 84 % is obsd. after 40 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo8m2Tz-bgctbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lig0RoNG5E2qA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXotlKitr4%253D&amp;md5=7022ee7000199c5c1f7e081822f23d7e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1016/S0016-0032(20)90061-3"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Washington, H.                         rightTab-cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Chemistry of the earth's crust</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Washington, Henry S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Franklin Institute</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">The number of essential rock-forming minerals is very small, and includes chiefly silicates of Al, Fe, Mg, Ca, Na, and K.  The av. igneous rock is approx. a granodiorite.  The 12 elements, O, Si, Al, Fe, Ca, Na, K, Mg, Ti, H, P, and Mn form 99.61% of the earth's crust.  The chem. elements fall into 2 groups, petrogenic and metallogenic.  The petrogenic elements are characteristic of and most abundant in the igneous rocks, have a low at. wt., and normally occur as oxides, silicates, chlorides, and fluorides.  The metallogenic elements are rare or absent in the igneous rocks, have a high at. wt., occur as ores (native metals, sulfides, arsenides, bromides, etc., but not primarily oxides or silicates).  It is suggested that beneath the silicate crust of petrogenic elements occurs first a zone essentially of Ni and Fe, then a central core of metallogenic elements.  In igneous rocks and minerals, the elements show a correlation in that certain of them are prone to occur with others.  Igneous rocks are distributed in comagmatic regions, i. e., regions of chem. related magmas.  Calcn. is made of the rock ds. from their chem. compn., also of the an. chem. compn. and ds. of the continental masses and oceanic floors.  The results show that the an. ds. of the continents, ocean floors, and various smaller regions of the earth stand in inverse relation to their elevations; these facts are confirmative of the theory of isostasy.  Attention is paid to the general characters of igneous rocks, the presence of gases and water vapor in the magma, and the analogy of the latter with a salt soln.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqQJSXxtxSHi7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lig0RoNG5E2qA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaB3MXitVSg&amp;md5=b28b823e5c870878d2e6153a34df8589</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref10" class="rightTabRefNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit10" data-doi="10.1016/j.coche.2015.08.007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dysart, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A comprehensive study is reported entailing optimization of sodium ion electrolyte formulation and compatibility studies with pos. and neg. electrode materials.  EC:PC:DMC and EC:PC:DME were found to exhibit optimum ionic conductivities and lower viscosities.  Yet, hard carbon neg. electrode materials tested in such electrolytes exhibit significant differences in performance, rooted in the different resistivity of the SEI, which results in too large polarization and concomitant loss of capacity at low potentials when DME is used as a co-solvent.  EC0.45:PC0.45:DMC0.1 was found to be the optimum compn. resulting in good rate capability and high capacity upon sustained cycling for hard carbon electrodes.  Its compatibility with pos. Na3V2(PO4)2F3 (NVPF) electrodes was also confirmed, which led to the assembly of full Na-ion cells displaying an operation voltage of 3.65 V, very low polarization and excellent capacity retention upon cycling with ca. 97 mA h g-1 of NVPF after more than 120 cycles together with satisfactory coulombic efficiency (&gt;98.5%) and very good power performance.
